    Explanation: When the solution consists of two disjoint intervals (like x < −3 or x > 3), we write them as a union: (−∞, −3) ∪ (3, ∞), meaning values in either interval satisfy the inequality.

  Q18. What does it mean when a quadratic inequality has 'no solution'?
    [ ] The roots are complex numbers
    [ ] The discriminant is zero
    [*] No real value of x makes the inequality true (e.g., x² + 1 < 0)
    [ ] The equation cannot be factored
    Explanation: A quadratic inequality has no solution when the expression never satisfies the inequality. For example, x² + 1 is always positive, so x² + 1 < 0 has no solution.

  Q19. What does it mean when the solution to a quadratic inequality is 'all real numbers'?
    [ ] The parabola crosses the x-axis
    [ ] The discriminant is positive
    [ ] The equation has two real roots
    [*] Every real value of x makes the inequality true (e.g., x² + 1 > 0)
    Explanation: If the quadratic expression is always positive (or always negative) and that matches the inequality direction, then every real number is a solution.

  Q20. What does 'boundary point' mean in solving inequalities?
    [*] A point where the expression equals zero, which may or may not be included in the solution depending on whether the inequality is strict (< >) or non-strict (≤ ≥)
    [ ] A point that is always excluded from the solution when considering all relevant factors
    [ ] The y-intercept of the graph, particularly regarding open following conventional understanding
    [ ] The maximum value of the expression, particularly regarding zero based on the prevailing evidence
    Explanation: Boundary points are where the expression equals zero. For ≤ or ≥, boundary points are included (closed circles); for < or >, they are excluded (open circles).

  Q21. A model rocket's height is h(t) = −16t² + 96t feet. During what time interval is the rocket above 80 feet?
    [ ] 2 < t < 4 seconds
    [*] 1 < t < 5 seconds
    [ ] 3 < t < 5 seconds
    [ ] 0 < t < 6 seconds
    Explanation: Set h > 80: −16t² + 96t > 80, so −16t² + 96t − 80 > 0, or t² − 6t + 5 < 0. Factor: (t − 1)(t − 5) < 0. Solution: 1 < t < 5 seconds.

  Q22. A company's profit is P(x) = −x² + 50x − 400 dollars. For what range of units sold is the company profitable (P > 0)?
    [*] Between 10 and 40 units
    [ ] Between 0 and 25 units
    [ ] Fewer than 10 units
    [ ] More than 50 units
    Explanation: Set P = 0: x² − 50x + 400 = 0. Factor: (x − 10)(x − 40) = 0. Since the parabola opens down, P > 0 between roots: 10 < x < 40.

  Q23. A ball is thrown upward from a 6-foot height with h(t) = −16t² + 32t + 6. During what time interval is the ball at or above 22 feet?
    [ ] Never reaches 22 feet
    [ ] 0.5 ≤ t ≤ 1.5 seconds
    [*] 1 ≤ t ≤ 1 second (only at t = 1)
    [ ] 0 ≤ t ≤ 2 seconds
    Explanation: Set h ≥ 22: −16t² + 32t + 6 ≥ 22, so −16t² + 32t − 16 ≥ 0, or t² − 2t + 1 ≤ 0. Factor: (t − 1)² ≤ 0. Since a square is always ≥ 0, equality holds only at t = 1. The ball reaches exactly 22 feet at t = 1 second.

  Q24. A farmer's crop yield (in bushels) is Y(x) = −2x² + 40x − 150, where x is pounds of fertilizer. For what range of fertilizer does the yield exceed 0?
    [ ] 0 < x < 20 pounds
    [ ] 10 < x < 30 pounds
    [ ] Any amount of fertilizer
    [*] 5 < x < 15 pounds
    Explanation: Set Y > 0: −2x² + 40x − 150 > 0, so x² − 20x + 75 < 0. Factor: (x − 5)(x − 15) < 0. Positive yield occurs when 5 < x < 15 pounds.

  Q25. A construction site requires that a crane's load remain below a certain stress level, modeled by S(x) = x² − 10x − 24. The load is safe when S(x) < 0. For what range of x is the load safe?
    [ ] 0 < x < 10
    [*] −2 < x < 12
    [ ] x < −2 or x > 12
    [ ] 2 < x < 24
    Explanation: Solve x² − 10x − 24 < 0. Factor: (x − 12)(x + 2) < 0. The expression is negative between roots: −2 < x < 12.

  Q26. A swimmer's speed S(t) = −t² + 6t − 5 (in m/s) varies with time. During what time interval is the swimmer's speed positive?
    [ ] 2 < t < 4 seconds
    [*] 1 < t < 5 seconds
    [ ] 0 < t < 6 seconds
    [ ] Never positive
    Explanation: Set S > 0: −t² + 6t − 5 > 0, so t² − 6t + 5 < 0. Factor: (t − 1)(t − 5) < 0. Speed is positive when 1 < t < 5 seconds.

  Q27. A concert hall's temperature change is modeled by T(h) = 2h² − 12h + 10, where h is hours after doors open. When is the temperature below the starting level (T < 0)?
    [ ] Never below starting level
    [ ] 2 < h < 4 hours
    [ ] 0 < h < 6 hours
    [*] 1 < h < 5 hours
    Explanation: Set T < 0: 2h² − 12h + 10 < 0, or h² − 6h + 5 < 0. Factor: (h − 1)(h − 5) < 0. Temperature is below starting level when 1 < h < 5 hours.

  Q28. A drainage pipe's water flow rate is F(d) = −d² + 8d − 12 gallons per minute, where d is the pipe diameter in inches. For which diameters is the flow rate positive?
    [ ] d > 6 inches
    [*] 2 < d < 6 inches
    [ ] 0 < d < 8 inches
    [ ] 4 < d < 8 inches
    Explanation: Set F > 0: −d² + 8d − 12 > 0, so d² − 8d + 12 < 0. Factor: (d − 2)(d − 6) < 0. Flow is positive for 2 < d < 6 inches.

  Q29. A stunt car jumps with height h(x) = −0.05x² + 2.5x − 20 feet at horizontal distance x feet. Over what range of horizontal distance is the car above ground (h > 0)?
    [*] Between x = 10 and x = 40 feet
    [ ] Between x = 20 and x = 30 feet
    [ ] Between x = 0 and x = 50 feet
    [ ] Between x = 5 and x = 45 feet
    Explanation: Set h > 0: −0.05x² + 2.5x − 20 > 0. Multiply by −20 (flip inequality): x² − 50x + 400 < 0. Factor: (x − 10)(x − 40) < 0. The car is above ground for 10 < x < 40 feet.

  Q30. An investment's return rate is modeled by R(t) = −t² + 14t − 40 percent, where t is years. During what years does the investment have a positive return?
    [*] Between years 4 and 10
    [ ] Between years 5 and 9
    [ ] Between years 0 and 14
    [ ] After year 7 only
    Explanation: Set R > 0: −t² + 14t − 40 > 0, so t² − 14t + 40 < 0. Factor: (t − 4)(t − 10) < 0. Positive return occurs when 4 < t < 10, or between years 4 and 10.

--- Lesson 2.7: Quadratic Inequalities (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Solve x² - 9 > 0
     A: x < -3 or x > 3. Parabola opens up, above x-axis outside roots.

  2. Q: Solve -x² + 4 ≤ 0
     A: x ≤ -2 or x ≥ 2. Opens down, below x-axis outside zeros.

  3. Q: Sign analysis: (x-1)(x+2) > 0
     A: x < -2 or x > 1. Test intervals: negative, zero, positive regions.

  4. Q: Inequality 2x² + 5x - 3 < 0
     A: -3 < x < 1/2. Factor: (2x-1)(x+3) < 0. Roots -3 and 1/2.

  5. Q: Quadratic inequality solution notation: x² ≥ 16
     A: x ≤ -4 or x ≥ 4. Interval: (-∞,-4] ∪ [4, ∞).

  6. Q: When does ax² + bx + c < 0 have no solution?
     A: When a > 0 and discriminant < 0 (parabola entirely above x-axis).

  7. Q: Solve (x-2)² ≥ 0
     A: All real x. Perfect square always ≥ 0.

  8. Q: Test point method: test x = 0 in x(x-5) > 0
     A: 0(0-5) = 0, not > 0. So x = 0 not in solution set.

  9. Q: Sign chart for x² - 4x - 5 = 0
     A: Roots: 5, -1. Chart: + (x<-1), - (-1<x<5), + (x>5).

  10. Q: Solution to 3x² ≤ 12x?
     A: 0 ≤ x ≤ 4. 3x² - 12x ≤ 0, 3x(x-4) ≤ 0, interval [0,4].

--- Lesson 3.1: Polynomial Operations (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Polynomial Operations
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the degree of (2x³ + x)(x² − 3)?
When multiplying polynomials, the degree of the product is the sum of the degrees: 3 + 2 = 5.
What is the leading coefficient of 7x⁵ − 3x³ + x − 9?
The leading coefficient is the coefficient of the highest-degree term. The highest degree is 5, and its coefficient is 7.
What is the constant term in (x − 2)(x + 3)(x − 1)?
The constant term is the product of the constant terms in each factor: (−2)(3)(−1) = 6.
What is a 'polynomial'?
A polynomial is a sum of terms, each of which is a constant multiplied by a variable raised to a non-negative integer power. Examples: 3x² + 2x − 5, x⁴ + 1.
What is a 'monomial'?
A monomial has exactly one term. A binomial has two terms, and a trinomial has three terms.
What is the 'difference of squares' pattern?
The difference of squares is a special product pattern: a² − b² always factors as (a − b)(a + b). Note that a² + b² does not factor over the reals.
Real-World Applications
Example: What does 'FOIL' stand for in polynomial multiplication?
FOIL stands for First, Outer, Inner, Last. It is a mnemonic for multiplying two binomials: multiply the First terms, Outer terms, Inner terms, and Last terms, then combine.
Example: What does 'distributive property' mean in polynomial multiplication?
The distributive property a(b + c) = ab + ac extends to polynomial multiplication: each term of the first polynomial multiplies every term of the second, then like terms are combined.

--- Lesson 3.1: Polynomial Operations (Quiz) ---

  Q1. (4x² + 3x − 2) + (2x² − 5x + 7) = ?
    [*] 6x² − 2x + 5
    [ ] 6x² − 2x − 5
    [ ] 2x² − 2x + 5
    [ ] 6x² + 8x + 5
    Explanation: Add like terms: 4x² + 2x² = 6x², 3x + (−5x) = −2x, −2 + 7 = 5. The result is 6x² − 2x + 5.

  Q2. (5x³ − x + 3) − (2x³ + 4x − 1) = ?
    [ ] 3x³ + 3x + 2
    [ ] 7x³ − 5x + 4
    [*] 3x³ − 5x + 4
    [ ] 3x³ − 5x + 2
    Explanation: Distribute the negative sign: 5x³ − 2x³ = 3x³, −x − 4x = −5x, 3 − (−1) = 4. The result is 3x³ − 5x + 4.

  Q3. (x + 4)(x − 5) = ?
    [ ] x² − x + 20
    [ ] x² + x − 20
    [*] x² − x − 20
    [ ] x² − 9x − 20
    Explanation: FOIL: x·x = x², x·(−5) = −5x, 4·x = 4x, 4·(−5) = −20. Combine: x² − 5x + 4x − 20 = x² − x − 20.

  Q4. (3x − 2)² = ?
    [ ] 9x² − 6x + 4
    [ ] 9x² − 4
    [*] 9x² − 12x + 4
    [ ] 9x² + 12x + 4
    Explanation: Using (a − b)² = a² − 2ab + b²: (3x)² − 2(3x)(2) + 2² = 9x² − 12x + 4.

  Q5. What is the degree of (2x³ + x)(x² − 3)?
    [ ] 2
    [ ] 6
    [ ] 3
    [*] 5
    Explanation: When multiplying polynomials, the degree of the product is the sum of the degrees: 3 + 2 = 5.

  Q6. What is the leading coefficient of 7x⁵ − 3x³ + x − 9?
    [ ] −3
    [ ] 5
    [ ] −9
    [*] 7
    Explanation: The leading coefficient is the coefficient of the highest-degree term. The highest degree is 5, and its coefficient is 7.

  Q7. (x + 1)(x² − x + 1) = ?
    [*] x³ + 1
    [ ] x³ + x² + 1
    [ ] x³ − 1
    [ ] x³ − x + 1
    Explanation: This is the sum of cubes pattern: (a + b)(a² − ab + b²) = a³ + b³. With a = x and b = 1: x³ + 1.

  Q8. (a + b)(a − b) = ?
    [*] a² − b²
    [ ] a² + 2ab + b²
    [ ] a² − 2ab + b²
    [ ] a² + b²
    Explanation: This is the difference of squares pattern. The middle terms cancel: a² − ab + ab − b² = a² − b².

  Q9. What is the constant term in (x − 2)(x + 3)(x − 1)?
    [ ] −3
    [ ] −6
    [*] 6
    [ ] 5
    Explanation: The constant term is the product of the constant terms in each factor: (−2)(3)(−1) = 6.

  Q10. Expand (x + 2)³.
    [*] x³ + 6x² + 12x + 8
    [ ] x³ + 2x² + 4x + 8
    [ ] x³ + 4x² + 6x + 8
    [ ] x³ + 8
    Explanation: Using (a + b)³ = a³ + 3a²b + 3ab² + b³: x³ + 3(x²)(2) + 3(x)(4) + 8 = x³ + 6x² + 12x + 8.

  Q11. What is a 'polynomial'?
    [ ] Any algebraic expression, including fractions with variables in the denominator
    [*] An expression consisting of variables and coefficients combined using addition, subtraction, and multiplication, with whole number exponents
    [ ] An equation set equal to zero
    [ ] An expression that always has exactly two terms
    Explanation: A polynomial is a sum of terms, each of which is a constant multiplied by a variable raised to a non-negative integer power. Examples: 3x² + 2x − 5, x⁴ + 1.

  Q12. What does 'degree of a polynomial' mean?
    [ ] The constant term of the polynomial
    [ ] The value of the leading coefficient
    [*] The highest power (exponent) of the variable in the polynomial
    [ ] The number of terms in the polynomial
    Explanation: The degree is the highest exponent of the variable. For example, 4x³ − 2x + 1 has degree 3. The degree determines the polynomial's basic shape and end behavior.

  Q13. What does 'leading coefficient' mean?
    [*] The coefficient of the term with the highest degree when the polynomial is in standard form
    [ ] The constant term
    [ ] The first coefficient written in the expression
    [ ] The largest coefficient in the polynomial
    Explanation: The leading coefficient is the coefficient of the highest-degree term. In 5x⁴ − 3x² + 1, the leading coefficient is 5 (from the x⁴ term).

  Q14. What does 'standard form' of a polynomial mean?
    [ ] Terms arranged by coefficient size
    [ ] Terms arranged from lowest degree to highest degree
    [*] Terms arranged in order from highest degree to lowest degree
    [ ] Terms arranged alphabetically
    Explanation: Standard form arranges terms in descending order of degree: highest exponent first, then the next highest, and so on, ending with the constant term.

  Q15. What is a 'monomial'?
    [ ] A polynomial with exactly two terms
    [ ] Any polynomial of degree one
    [ ] A polynomial with exactly three terms
    [*] A polynomial with exactly one term (like 5x³ or −7)
    Explanation: A monomial has exactly one term. A binomial has two terms, and a trinomial has three terms.

  Q16. What does 'like terms' mean in polynomial operations?
    [ ] Terms that are both positive
    [ ] Terms that have the same coefficient
    [*] Terms that have the same variable(s) raised to the same power(s)
    [ ] Terms that are next to each other
    Explanation: Like terms have identical variable parts. For example, 3x² and −7x² are like terms (both have x²), but 3x² and 3x are not.

  Q17. What does 'FOIL' stand for in polynomial multiplication?
    [ ] First, Only, Inner, Limit
    [ ] Factor, Organize, Integrate, Link
    [ ] Find, Order, Identify, List
    [*] First, Outer, Inner, Last — a method for multiplying two binomials
    Explanation: FOIL stands for First, Outer, Inner, Last. It is a mnemonic for multiplying two binomials: multiply the First terms, Outer terms, Inner terms, and Last terms, then combine.

  Q18. What is the 'difference of squares' pattern?
    [ ] a² − b² = (a − b)²
    [ ] a² + b² = (a + b)(a − b)
    [ ] a² − b² = a(a − b²)
    [*] a² − b² = (a − b)(a + b)
    Explanation: The difference of squares is a special product pattern: a² − b² always factors as (a − b)(a + b). Note that a² + b² does not factor over the reals.

  Q19. What does 'cubic polynomial' mean?
    [ ] A polynomial that can be factored into 3 parts
    [ ] A polynomial whose coefficients are all cubes
    [ ] A polynomial with exactly 3 terms
    [*] A polynomial of degree 3, with the highest term being x³
    Explanation: A cubic polynomial has degree 3, meaning its highest-power term is x³. Examples include x³ − 2x + 1 and 5x³ + x² − 3x.

  Q20. What does 'distributive property' mean in polynomial multiplication?
    [*] Each term in one polynomial must be multiplied by every term in the other polynomial
    [ ] You can only multiply monomials
    [ ] The order of multiplication does not matter
    [ ] Terms can only be added if they are like terms
    Explanation: The distributive property a(b + c) = ab + ac extends to polynomial multiplication: each term of the first polynomial multiplies every term of the second, then like terms are combined.

  Q21. A box has dimensions (x + 2), (x − 1), and (x + 3) inches. What polynomial represents the volume of the box?
    [ ] x³ + 4x² − x − 6
    [ ] x³ + 6x² + 11x + 6
    [*] x³ + 4x² + x − 6
    [ ] 3x + 4
    Explanation: V = (x+2)(x−1)(x+3). First: (x+2)(x−1) = x²+x−2. Then: (x²+x−2)(x+3) = x³+3x²+x²+3x−2x−6 = x³+4x²+x−6.

  Q22. A company's revenue from selling x items is R(x) = x(50 − 2x) dollars, and the cost is C(x) = 10x + 60 dollars. What polynomial represents the profit P(x) = R(x) − C(x)?
    [*] −2x² + 40x − 60
    [ ] −2x² + 60x − 60
    [ ] −2x² + 40x + 60
    [ ] 2x² + 40x + 60
    Explanation: R(x) = 50x − 2x². P(x) = (50x − 2x²) − (10x + 60) = −2x² + 50x − 10x − 60 = −2x² + 40x − 60.

  Q23. A rectangular garden has length (2x + 5) meters and width (x + 3) meters. What polynomial represents the area of the garden?
    [ ] 2x² + 8x + 15
    [*] 2x² + 11x + 15
    [ ] 2x² + 11x + 8
    [ ] 3x + 8
    Explanation: Area = length × width = (2x + 5)(x + 3) = 2x² + 6x + 5x + 15 = 2x² + 11x + 15 square meters.

  Q24. A cylindrical tank has radius (x + 1) and height (x + 4). Using V = πr²h, what polynomial (without π) represents the volume?
    [ ] x² + 2x + 1
    [*] x³ + 6x² + 9x + 4
    [ ] x³ + 5x² + 4x + 4
    [ ] x³ + 4x² + x + 4
    Explanation: r² = (x+1)² = x²+2x+1. V/π = (x²+2x+1)(x+4) = x³+4x²+2x²+8x+x+4 = x³+6x²+9x+4.

  Q25. Two investors' portfolios grow as P₁(t) = 3t² + 5t + 100 and P₂(t) = 2t² − 3t + 200 dollars after t years. What polynomial represents their combined portfolio value?
    [ ] 5t² + 8t + 300
    [ ] t² + 8t + 300
    [ ] 6t² + 2t + 300
    [*] 5t² + 2t + 300
    Explanation: Add the polynomials: (3t² + 2t²) + (5t − 3t) + (100 + 200) = 5t² + 2t + 300.

  Q26. A farmer's crop yield per acre is (x + 10) bushels and he farms (x² − 4) acres. What polynomial represents his total yield?
    [ ] x³ − 10x² − 4x + 40
    [*] x³ + 10x² − 4x − 40
    [ ] x² + 10x − 4
    [ ] x³ + 10x² + 4x − 40
    Explanation: Total yield = (x + 10)(x² − 4) = x³ − 4x + 10x² − 40 = x³ + 10x² − 4x − 40.

  Q27. A store's monthly revenue is R(x) = −x² + 120x and monthly expenses increase from E₁(x) = 50x to E₂(x) = 55x + 100. By how much does the profit polynomial decrease?
    [*] 5x + 100
    [ ] 5x − 100
    [ ] −5x + 100
    [ ] 50x + 100
    Explanation: Old profit: R − E₁ = −x² + 70x. New profit: R − E₂ = −x² + 65x − 100. Decrease = old − new = (−x² + 70x) − (−x² + 65x − 100) = 5x + 100.

  Q28. An engineer models the cross-sectional area of a beam as (3x − 1)(x + 2) cm². If the beam is (2x + 5) cm long, what polynomial gives the volume?
    [ ] 5x² + 5x − 2
    [ ] 6x³ + 19x² + 3x − 10
    [*] 6x³ + 25x² + 21x − 10
    [ ] 6x³ + 15x² − 2x − 10
    Explanation: First: (3x−1)(x+2) = 3x²+5x−2. Then: (3x²+5x−2)(2x+5) = 6x³+15x²+10x²+25x−4x−10 = 6x³+25x²+21x−10.

  Q29. A population model is P(t) = t³ + 5t² + 100 at the start of a year and Q(t) = t³ + 5t² + 8t + 100 at the end. What polynomial represents the population growth during the year?
    [ ] 2t³ + 10t² + 8t + 200
    [ ] t² + 8t
    [*] 8t
    [ ] −8t
    Explanation: Growth = Q(t) − P(t) = (t³+5t²+8t+100) − (t³+5t²+100) = 8t.

  Q30. A picture frame has outer dimensions (x + 6) by (x + 4) inches and inner dimensions (x + 2) by x inches. What polynomial represents the area of the frame only?
    [ ] 10x + 24
    [*] 8x + 24
    [ ] x² + 8x + 24
    [ ] 2x² + 10x + 24
    Explanation: Outer area: (x+6)(x+4) = x²+10x+24. Inner area: x(x+2) = x²+2x. Frame area = (x²+10x+24) − (x²+2x) = 8x + 24.

--- Lesson 3.1: Polynomial Operations (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Add (3x² + 2x - 5) + (x² - 3x + 7)
     A: 4x² - x + 2. Combine like terms.

  2. Q: Subtract (2x³ - x) - (x³ + 2x - 1)
     A: x³ - 3x + 1. Distribute negative, combine like terms.

  3. Q: Multiply (x + 3)(x - 2)
     A: x² + x - 6. FOIL: x² - 2x + 3x - 6.

  4. Q: Expand (2x + 1)²
     A: 4x² + 4x + 1. (a+b)² = a² + 2ab + b².

  5. Q: Multiply (x² + 2x + 1)(x - 1)
     A: x³ + x² - 1. Use distributive property.

  6. Q: Divide 6x³ + 9x² by 3x
     A: 2x² + 3x. Divide each term.

  7. Q: Degree of (x² - 1)(x³ + x)
     A: Degree 5. Degrees add: 2 + 3 = 5.

  8. Q: Leading coefficient of 5x⁴ - 3x² + 2
     A: 5. Coefficient of highest degree term.

  9. Q: Subtract 2x² from 5x²
     A: 3x². Same variable, same power.

  10. Q: Simplify (3x²y)(2xy²)
     A: 6x³y³. Multiply coefficients and variables (add powers).

--- Lesson 3.2: Factoring Polynomials (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Factoring Polynomials
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the GCF of 8x⁴ + 12x³ − 4x²?
The GCF of 8, 12, and 4 is 4. The lowest power of x is x². So the GCF is 4x².
What is the 'Greatest Common Factor' (GCF) in factoring?
The GCF is the largest monomial that is a factor of every term. It includes both the GCF of the coefficients and the lowest power of each common variable.
What is the 'difference of squares' factoring pattern?
The difference of squares pattern states that a² − b² = (a − b)(a + b). The two factors are conjugates — they are identical except one uses + and the other uses −.
What is the 'difference of cubes' formula?
The difference of cubes factors as a³ − b³ = (a − b)(a² + ab + b²). Note the signs: same, opposite, always positive (SOAP).
What is the 'sum of cubes' formula?
The sum of cubes factors as a³ + b³ = (a + b)(a² − ab + b²). Using SOAP: the binomial has the Same sign as the original, the linear term is Opposite, and the last term is Always Positive.
What is a 'perfect square trinomial'?
A perfect square trinomial has the form a² + 2ab + b² = (a + b)² or a² − 2ab + b² = (a − b)². The middle term is always twice the product of the square roots of the first and last terms.
What is the 'AC method' for factoring trinomials?
The AC method for ax² + bx + c: multiply a × c, find two numbers that multiply to ac and add to b, split the middle term, then factor by grouping.
A rectangular garden has area x² + 9x + 20 square meters. If the width is (x + 4) meters, what is the length?
Factor the area: x² + 9x + 20 = (x + 4)(x + 5). Since width = (x + 4), the length = (x + 5) meters.
A parking lot has total area 2x² + 14x + 24 square meters. If one dimension is 2(x + 3), what is the other?
Factor: 2x² + 14x + 24 = 2(x² + 7x + 12) = 2(x + 3)(x + 4). Since one dimension is 2(x + 3), the other is (x + 4).
An architect designs a window with area x² − 6x + 9 square feet. She recognizes this is a perfect square. What is the side length of the square window?
x² − 6x + 9 = (x − 3)². Since this is a perfect square, the window is a square with side length (x − 3) feet.
Real-World Applications
Example: A storage container has volume 6x³ + 12x² + 6x cubic feet. Factor this completely to find the dimensions.
Factor out GCF: 6x(x² + 2x + 1). Recognize the perfect square trinomial: 6x(x + 1)².

--- Lesson 3.2: Factoring Polynomials (Quiz) ---

  Q1. Factor x² + 7x + 12.
    [*] (x + 3)(x + 4)
    [ ] (x + 1)(x + 12)
    [ ] (x + 2)(x + 6)
    [ ] (x − 3)(x − 4)
    Explanation: Find two numbers that multiply to 12 and add to 7: 3 and 4. So x² + 7x + 12 = (x + 3)(x + 4).

  Q2. Factor x² − 25.
    [*] (x − 5)(x + 5)
    [ ] (x + 5)²
    [ ] Cannot be factored
    [ ] (x − 5)²
    Explanation: This is a difference of squares: a² − b² = (a − b)(a + b). Here a = x, b = 5, so x² − 25 = (x − 5)(x + 5).

  Q3. What is the GCF of 8x⁴ + 12x³ − 4x²?
    [ ] 4x
    [*] 4x²
    [ ] 8x²
    [ ] 2x
    Explanation: The GCF of 8, 12, and 4 is 4. The lowest power of x is x². So the GCF is 4x².

  Q4. Factor 3x² − 10x − 8.
    [*] (3x + 2)(x − 4)
    [ ] (x − 2)(3x + 4)
    [ ] (3x − 2)(x + 4)
    [ ] (3x − 8)(x + 1)
    Explanation: AC method: 3 × (−8) = −24. Find factors of −24 that sum to −10: −12 and 2. Rewrite: 3x² − 12x + 2x − 8 = 3x(x − 4) + 2(x − 4) = (3x + 2)(x − 4).

  Q5. Factor x³ − 8.
    [ ] (x + 2)(x² − 2x + 4)
    [ ] (x − 2)³
    [ ] (x − 2)(x² − 2x + 4)
    [*] (x − 2)(x² + 2x + 4)
    Explanation: Difference of cubes: a³ − b³ = (a − b)(a² + ab + b²). With a = x, b = 2: (x − 2)(x² + 2x + 4).

  Q6. Factor x³ + 64.
    [ ] (x − 4)(x² + 4x + 16)
    [*] (x + 4)(x² − 4x + 16)
    [ ] (x + 4)(x² + 4x + 16)
    [ ] (x + 4)³
    Explanation: Sum of cubes: a³ + b³ = (a + b)(a² − ab + b²). With a = x, b = 4: (x + 4)(x² − 4x + 16).

  Q7. Factor by grouping: x³ + 3x² + 5x + 15.
    [ ] (x² + 3)(x + 5)
    [ ] Cannot be factored
    [ ] (x + 3)(x + 5)
    [*] (x² + 5)(x + 3)
    Explanation: Group: x²(x + 3) + 5(x + 3) = (x² + 5)(x + 3).

  Q8. Factor completely: 2x³ − 18x.
    [*] 2x(x − 3)(x + 3)
    [ ] 2(x³ − 9x)
    [ ] x(2x − 18)
    [ ] 2x(x² − 9)
    Explanation: First factor out the GCF: 2x(x² − 9). Then factor the difference of squares: 2x(x − 3)(x + 3).

  Q9. Factor 9x² − 12x + 4.
    [ ] (3x + 2)²
    [ ] (9x − 4)(x − 1)
    [*] (3x − 2)²
    [ ] (3x − 2)(3x + 2)
    Explanation: This is a perfect square trinomial: (3x)² − 2(3x)(2) + 2² = (3x − 2)².

  Q10. Is x² + 9 factorable over the real numbers?
    [ ] Yes, (x + 3i)(x − 3i)
    [*] No, the sum of squares cannot be factored over the reals
    [ ] Yes, (x + 3)(x + 3)
    [ ] Yes, (x − 3)(x + 3)
    Explanation: The sum of squares a² + b² does not factor over the real numbers. It can only be factored using complex numbers: (x + 3i)(x − 3i).

  Q11. What does 'factoring a polynomial' mean?
    [ ] Evaluating the polynomial at a specific value
    [ ] Finding the degree of the polynomial
    [ ] Expanding a polynomial into more terms
    [*] Writing a polynomial as a product of simpler polynomials (its factors)
    Explanation: Factoring is the reverse of expanding. It rewrites a polynomial as a product of two or more polynomials of lower degree.

  Q12. What is the 'Greatest Common Factor' (GCF) in factoring?
    [*] The largest expression (including both numerical and variable parts) that divides evenly into all terms of the polynomial
    [ ] The term with the highest degree, particularly regarding term under the given assumptions
    [ ] The largest coefficient in the polynomial based on established principles
    [ ] The smallest term in the polynomial, particularly regarding what as predicted by standard models
    Explanation: The GCF is the largest monomial that is a factor of every term. It includes both the GCF of the coefficients and the lowest power of each common variable.

  Q13. What is the 'difference of squares' factoring pattern?
    [ ] a² + b² = (a − b)(a + b) as predicted by standard models
    [ ] a² − b² = a(a − b²) in the context of this analysis
    [ ] a² − b² = (a − b)², particularly regarding
    [*] a² − b² = (a − b)(a + b) — it factors into conjugate binomials
    Explanation: The difference of squares pattern states that a² − b² = (a − b)(a + b). The two factors are conjugates — they are identical except one uses + and the other uses −.

  Q14. What does 'factoring by grouping' mean?
    [ ] Grouping all constant terms together, particularly regarding polynomial when considering all relevant factors
    [ ] Dividing the polynomial by its degree, particularly regarding splits following the established framework
    [*] A method for factoring four-term polynomials by grouping into pairs, factoring each pair, then extracting the common binomial factor
    [ ] Sorting terms by degree, particularly regarding mean when measured under controlled conditions
    Explanation: Factoring by grouping splits a polynomial (usually 4 terms) into two groups, factors each group separately, and then factors out the common binomial factor.

  Q15. What is the 'difference of cubes' formula?
    [ ] a³ − b³ = (a + b)(a² + ab + b²)
    [*] a³ − b³ = (a − b)(a² + ab + b²)
    [ ] a³ − b³ = (a − b)(a² − ab + b²)
    [ ] a³ − b³ = (a − b)³
    Explanation: The difference of cubes factors as a³ − b³ = (a − b)(a² + ab + b²). Note the signs: same, opposite, always positive (SOAP).

  Q16. What is the 'sum of cubes' formula?
    [*] a³ + b³ = (a + b)(a² − ab + b²)
    [ ] a³ + b³ = (a + b)(a² + ab + b²)
    [ ] a³ + b³ = (a + b)³
    [ ] a³ + b³ = (a − b)(a² − ab + b²)
    Explanation: The sum of cubes factors as a³ + b³ = (a + b)(a² − ab + b²). Using SOAP: the binomial has the Same sign as the original, the linear term is Opposite, and the last term is Always Positive.

  Q17. What does 'factor completely' mean?
    [*] Continue factoring until no factor can be factored further (all factors are prime/irreducible)
    [ ] Use only the difference of squares pattern
    [ ] Factor into exactly two factors
    [ ] Factor out only the GCF
    Explanation: To factor completely, you must continue factoring each factor until all remaining factors are prime (cannot be factored further over the integers).

  Q18. What is a 'perfect square trinomial'?
    [*] A trinomial of the form a² ± 2ab + b² that factors as (a ± b)²
    [ ] Any trinomial with three terms
    [ ] A trinomial where all coefficients are perfect squares
    [ ] A trinomial that equals zero
    Explanation: A perfect square trinomial has the form a² + 2ab + b² = (a + b)² or a² − 2ab + b² = (a − b)². The middle term is always twice the product of the square roots of the first and last terms.

  Q19. What does 'prime polynomial' (or 'irreducible polynomial') mean?
    [*] A polynomial that cannot be factored into polynomials of lower degree with integer coefficients
    [ ] A polynomial of degree 1
    [ ] A polynomial with only one term
    [ ] A polynomial whose leading coefficient is a prime number
    Explanation: A prime (irreducible) polynomial cannot be factored further over a given number system. For example, x² + 1 is prime over the reals but factors as (x + i)(x − i) over the complex numbers.

  Q20. What is the 'AC method' for factoring trinomials?
    [ ] Dividing the polynomial by a and c
    [*] For ax² + bx + c, multiply a and c, find factors of ac that sum to b, then use grouping to factor
    [ ] Using the quadratic formula instead of factoring
    [ ] Adding a and c to find the middle term
    Explanation: The AC method for ax² + bx + c: multiply a × c, find two numbers that multiply to ac and add to b, split the middle term, then factor by grouping.

  Q21. A rectangular garden has area x² + 9x + 20 square meters. If the width is (x + 4) meters, what is the length?
    [ ] (x + 2) meters
    [ ] (x + 10) meters
    [ ] (x + 4) meters
    [*] (x + 5) meters
    Explanation: Factor the area: x² + 9x + 20 = (x + 4)(x + 5). Since width = (x + 4), the length = (x + 5) meters.

  Q22. A ball's height is h(t) = −16t² + 48t. By factoring, when does the ball hit the ground?
    [ ] At t = 48 seconds
    [ ] At t = 2 seconds
    [ ] At t = 4 seconds
    [*] At t = 3 seconds
    Explanation: Set h = 0: −16t² + 48t = 0. Factor: −16t(t − 3) = 0. So t = 0 (launch) or t = 3 seconds (landing).

  Q23. A company's profit is P(x) = x² − 2x − 35. By factoring, at what quantities does the company break even?
    [ ] x = 5 and x = −7
    [*] x = 7 and x = −5
    [ ] x = 35 and x = −1
    [ ] x = 7 and x = 5
    Explanation: Set P = 0: x² − 2x − 35 = 0. Factor: (x − 7)(x + 5) = 0. Break-even at x = 7 and x = −5 (only x = 7 is physically meaningful).

  Q24. An ice cube with side length x cm has volume x³. After melting to a smaller cube, the remaining solid volume is x³ − 27. By factoring, express this as a product.
    [*] (x − 3)(x² + 3x + 9)
    [ ] (x − 27)(x + 1)
    [ ] (x + 3)(x² − 3x + 9)
    [ ] (x − 3)³
    Explanation: x³ − 27 is a difference of cubes: x³ − 3³ = (x − 3)(x² + 3x + 9).

  Q25. A floor tile has area 4x² − 12x + 9 square inches. What are the dimensions of this square tile?
    [ ] (2x + 3) by (2x + 3) inches
    [ ] (4x − 3) by (x − 3) inches
    [*] (2x − 3) by (2x − 3) inches
    [ ] (2x − 9) by (2x − 1) inches
    Explanation: 4x² − 12x + 9 = (2x)² − 2(2x)(3) + 3² = (2x − 3)². The tile is a square with side length (2x − 3) inches.

  Q26. A storage container has volume 6x³ + 12x² + 6x cubic feet. Factor this completely to find the dimensions.
    [*] 6x(x + 1)²
    [ ] 2x(3x + 3)²
    [ ] 6(x³ + 2x² + x)
    [ ] 6x(x² + 2x + 1)
    Explanation: Factor out GCF: 6x(x² + 2x + 1). Recognize the perfect square trinomial: 6x(x + 1)².

  Q27. A bridge support beam has cross-section x⁴ − 16 cm². Factor this completely to analyze stress distribution.
    [ ] (x − 4)(x + 4)
    [ ] (x² − 4)(x² + 4)
    [*] (x − 2)(x + 2)(x² + 4)
    [ ] (x − 2)²(x + 2)²
    Explanation: x⁴ − 16 = (x²)² − 4² = (x² − 4)(x² + 4) = (x − 2)(x + 2)(x² + 4). Note that x² + 4 cannot be factored further over the reals.

  Q28. A parking lot has total area 2x² + 14x + 24 square meters. If one dimension is 2(x + 3), what is the other?
    [ ] (x + 8) meters
    [ ] (2x + 4) meters
    [*] (x + 4) meters
    [ ] (x + 3) meters
    Explanation: Factor: 2x² + 14x + 24 = 2(x² + 7x + 12) = 2(x + 3)(x + 4). Since one dimension is 2(x + 3), the other is (x + 4).

  Q29. A physics equation involves the expression 8t³ + 27. Factor this to simplify the equation.
    [*] (2t + 3)(4t² − 6t + 9)
    [ ] (8t + 27)(t − 1)
    [ ] (2t + 3)³
    [ ] (2t − 3)(4t² + 6t + 9)
    Explanation: 8t³ + 27 = (2t)³ + 3³. Sum of cubes: (2t + 3)((2t)² − (2t)(3) + 3²) = (2t + 3)(4t² − 6t + 9).

  Q30. An architect designs a window with area x² − 6x + 9 square feet. She recognizes this is a perfect square. What is the side length of the square window?
    [*] (x − 3) feet
    [ ] (x − 6) feet
    [ ] (x + 3) feet
    [ ] (x − 9) feet
    Explanation: x² − 6x + 9 = (x − 3)². Since this is a perfect square, the window is a square with side length (x − 3) feet.

--- Lesson 3.2: Factoring Polynomials (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Factor x² + 5x + 6
     A: (x + 2)(x + 3). Two numbers: 2, 3 sum to 5, product 6.

  2. Q: Factor 2x² - 11x + 5
     A: (2x - 1)(x - 5). AC method: a·c = 10, pair 1·10.

  3. Q: Factor x² - 16
     A: (x - 4)(x + 4). Difference of squares: a² - b² = (a-b)(a+b).

  4. Q: GCF of 12x³ + 18x²
     A: 6x². Largest common factor.

  5. Q: Factor 3x² + 12
     A: 3(x² + 4). Cannot factor further (sum of squares).

  6. Q: Factor by grouping: xy + xz + wy + wz
     A: (y + z)(x + w). Group: x(y+z) + w(y+z).

  7. Q: Factor x³ - 27
     A: (x - 3)(x² + 3x + 9). Difference of cubes: a³ - b³ = (a-b)(a²+ab+b²).

  8. Q: Perfect square trinomial x² + 8x + 16
     A: (x + 4)². Form (a+b)² = a² + 2ab + b².

  9. Q: Factor 5x² - 20
     A: 5(x² - 4) = 5(x-2)(x+2).

  10. Q: Factor x⁴ - 1
     A: (x² - 1)(x² + 1) = (x-1)(x+1)(x²+1). Difference of squares twice.

--- Lesson 3.3: Synthetic Division (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Synthetic Division
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
Divide (x³ + 2x² − 5x + 1) by (x − 1) using synthetic division. What is the quotient and remainder?
Synthetic division with c = 1 and coefficients [1, 2, −5, 1]: 1; 1+2=3; 3−5=−2; −2+1=−1. Quotient: x² + 3x − 2, remainder −1.
Divide (x⁴ − 1) by (x − 1). What is the quotient?
Use coefficients [1, 0, 0, 0, −1] with c = 1. Process gives [1, 1, 1, 1, 0]. Quotient: x³ + x² + x + 1, remainder 0.
Divide (x³ − 8) by (x − 2). What is the quotient?
Use coefficients [1, 0, 0, −8] with c = 2. Process: 1; 2+0=2; 4+0=4; 8+(−8)=0. Quotient: x² + 2x + 4, remainder 0.
Divide (2x² + 5x − 3) by (x + 3). What is the quotient and remainder?
Synthetic division with c = −3: [2, 5, −3]. Process: 2; 2(−3)+5=−1; (−1)(−3)+(−3)=0. Quotient: 2x − 1, remainder 0.
Divide (3x³ − 2x² + x − 6) by (x − 2). What is the remainder?
Synthetic division with c = 2: [3, −2, 1, −6]. Process: 3; 6+(−2)=4; 8+1=9; 18+(−6)=12. Remainder = 12.
What is 'synthetic division'?
Synthetic division is an efficient shortcut for polynomial long division when the divisor is (x − c). It uses only the coefficients and is faster than traditional long division.
What is the 'Remainder Theorem'?
The Remainder Theorem states that when f(x) is divided by (x − c), the remainder equals f(c). This provides a quick way to evaluate polynomials.
What is the 'Factor Theorem'?
The Factor Theorem is a consequence of the Remainder Theorem: if f(c) = 0, then the remainder is 0, meaning (x − c) divides f(x) evenly and is therefore a factor.
What is 'polynomial long division'?
Polynomial long division works for any polynomial divisor. The steps are: divide the leading terms, multiply, subtract, bring down, and repeat.
An engineer tests if a load value x = 2 is a critical point for stress function f(x) = x³ − 4x² + x + 6. Using synthetic division, what is f(2)?
Synthetic division with c = 2 and [1, −4, 1, 6]: 1; 2+(−4)=−2; −4+1=−3; −6+6=0. Remainder = 0, so f(2) = 0 and x = 2 is a critical point.
A student divides f(x) = x⁴ + 3x³ − x − 3 by (x + 3). What is the depressed (quotient) polynomial?
Synthetic division with c = −3 and [1, 3, 0, −1, −3]: 1; −3+3=0; 0+0=0; 0+(−1)=−1; 3+(−3)=0. Quotient: x³ − 1, remainder 0.
A bridge load function is f(x) = x³ − 6x² + 11x − 6. After dividing by (x − 1), what is the quotient that reveals the remaining factors?
Synthetic division with c = 1 and [1, −6, 11, −6]: 1; 1+(−6)=−5; −5+11=6; 6+(−6)=0. Quotient: x² − 5x + 6 = (x − 2)(x − 3).
A chemist's reaction rate is modeled by R(t) = t⁴ − 5t³ + 5t² + 5t − 6. She knows t = 1 and t = 2 are roots. Using successive synthetic division, what is the remaining quadratic factor?
Divide by (t − 1): [1, −5, 5, 5, −6] gives quotient t³ − 4t² + t + 6. Divide by (t − 2): [1, −4, 1, 6] gives quotient t² − 2t − 3 = (t − 3)(t + 1). Full: (t − 1)(t − 2)(t − 3)(t + 1).
Real-World Applications
Example: A cost function is C(x) = x³ + 3x² − 10x − 24. A manager suspects (x + 2) is a factor. Is this correct?
Synthetic division with c = −2 and [1, 3, −10, −24]: 1; −2+3=1; −2+(−10)=−12; 24+(−24)=0. Remainder = 0 confirms (x + 2) is a factor.

--- Lesson 3.3: Synthetic Division (Quiz) ---

  Q1. Divide (x³ + 2x² − 5x + 1) by (x − 1) using synthetic division. What is the quotient and remainder?
    [ ] x² + 2x − 5, remainder 1
    [ ] x² + 3x − 2, remainder 1
    [*] x² + 3x − 2, remainder −1
    [ ] x² + x − 4, remainder 5
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 1 and coefficients [1, 2, −5, 1]: 1; 1+2=3; 3−5=−2; −2+1=−1. Quotient: x² + 3x − 2, remainder −1.

  Q2. When dividing by (x + 3), what value of c do you use in synthetic division?
    [*] −3
    [ ] 1/3
    [ ] 3
    [ ] −1/3
    Explanation: In synthetic division by (x − c), you use c. Since x + 3 = x − (−3), you use c = −3.

  Q3. Divide (x⁴ − 1) by (x − 1). What is the quotient?
    [ ] x³ + 1
    [*] x³ + x² + x + 1
    [ ] x³ − x² + x − 1
    [ ] x³ − 1
    Explanation: Use coefficients [1, 0, 0, 0, −1] with c = 1. Process gives [1, 1, 1, 1, 0]. Quotient: x³ + x² + x + 1, remainder 0.

  Q4. If the remainder of f(x) ÷ (x − 2) is 0, what does this tell you?
    [ ] The quotient is zero
    [ ] f(x) has no real roots
    [ ] x = −2 is a root
    [*] x = 2 is a root of f(x) and (x − 2) is a factor
    Explanation: A remainder of 0 means (x − 2) divides evenly into f(x), so (x − 2) is a factor and x = 2 is a root (by the Factor Theorem).

  Q5. Divide (x³ − 8) by (x − 2). What is the quotient?
    [ ] x² + 2x − 4
    [ ] x² − 2x + 4
    [*] x² + 2x + 4
    [ ] x² + 4
    Explanation: Use coefficients [1, 0, 0, −8] with c = 2. Process: 1; 2+0=2; 4+0=4; 8+(−8)=0. Quotient: x² + 2x + 4, remainder 0.

  Q6. What must you do if a term is missing when setting up synthetic division?
    [*] Use 0 as a placeholder for the missing term's coefficient
    [ ] Synthetic division cannot be used
    [ ] Use 1 as a placeholder
    [ ] Skip that column
    Explanation: When a term is missing, you must include 0 as its coefficient placeholder. For example, x³ − 5 has coefficients [1, 0, 0, −5].

  Q7. Synthetic division can only be used when dividing by a divisor of what form?
    [ ] Any quadratic, particularly regarding
    [ ] A monomial, particularly regarding
    [ ] Any polynomial, particularly regarding
    [*] (x − c), a linear binomial with leading coefficient 1
    Explanation: Synthetic division is a shortcut that only works for divisors of the form (x − c), where the coefficient of x is 1.

  Q8. Divide (2x² + 5x − 3) by (x + 3). What is the quotient and remainder?
    [*] 2x − 1, remainder 0
    [ ] 2x − 1, remainder 3
    [ ] 2x + 1, remainder 0
    [ ] 2x + 3, remainder −3
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = −3: [2, 5, −3]. Process: 2; 2(−3)+5=−1; (−1)(−3)+(−3)=0. Quotient: 2x − 1, remainder 0.

  Q9. Use synthetic division to evaluate f(3) for f(x) = x³ − 2x² + x − 5.
    [ ] 1
    [ ] 13
    [ ] −5
    [*] 7
    Explanation: By the Remainder Theorem, f(3) equals the remainder when dividing by (x − 3). Process: 1; 3+(−2)=1; 3+1=4; 12+(−5)=7. So f(3) = 7.

  Q10. Divide (3x³ − 2x² + x − 6) by (x − 2). What is the remainder?
    [*] 12
    [ ] 8
    [ ] −6
    [ ] 0
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 2: [3, −2, 1, −6]. Process: 3; 6+(−2)=4; 8+1=9; 18+(−6)=12. Remainder = 12.

  Q11. What is 'synthetic division'?
    [ ] A way to multiply polynomials quickly
    [*] A shortcut method for dividing a polynomial by a linear factor (x − c) using only the coefficients
    [ ] A method for factoring any polynomial
    [ ] A method for finding the GCF
    Explanation: Synthetic division is an efficient shortcut for polynomial long division when the divisor is (x − c). It uses only the coefficients and is faster than traditional long division.

  Q12. What does 'dividend' mean in polynomial division?
    [ ] The polynomial you are dividing by
    [ ] The result of the division
    [*] The polynomial being divided (the numerator)
    [ ] The leftover after division
    Explanation: The dividend is the polynomial being divided. In f(x) ÷ g(x), f(x) is the dividend and g(x) is the divisor.

  Q13. What does 'quotient' mean in polynomial division?
    [ ] The original polynomial, particularly regarding does as determined by standard methodology
    [ ] The divisor, particularly regarding dividend as observed in standard experiments
    [ ] The leftover portion after division, particularly regarding algorithm based on the prevailing evidence
    [*] The result of the division — the polynomial that, multiplied by the divisor and added to the remainder, gives the dividend
    Explanation: The quotient is the result of division. The division algorithm states: Dividend = Divisor times Quotient + Remainder.

  Q14. What does 'remainder' mean in polynomial division?
    [ ] Always zero, particularly regarding constant as predicted by standard models
    [*] The part of the dividend left over after division, with degree less than the divisor
    [ ] The quotient of the division in most standard conditions
    [ ] The leading coefficient of the quotient
    Explanation: The remainder is what is left over. When dividing by (x − c), the remainder is a constant. If the remainder is 0, the divisor is a factor of the dividend.

  Q15. What is the 'Remainder Theorem'?
    [ ] The remainder equals the degree of f(x)
    [*] When f(x) is divided by (x − c), the remainder equals f(c)
    [ ] The remainder is always positive
    [ ] The remainder is always the leading coefficient
    Explanation: The Remainder Theorem states that when f(x) is divided by (x − c), the remainder equals f(c). This provides a quick way to evaluate polynomials.

  Q16. What is the 'Factor Theorem'?
    [*] (x − c) is a factor of f(x) if and only if f(c) = 0
    [ ] The GCF of any polynomial is always 1
    [ ] Every polynomial can be factored into linear factors
    [ ] A polynomial of degree n has exactly n factors
    Explanation: The Factor Theorem is a consequence of the Remainder Theorem: if f(c) = 0, then the remainder is 0, meaning (x − c) divides f(x) evenly and is therefore a factor.

  Q17. What does 'depressed polynomial' mean in synthetic division?
    [*] The quotient polynomial, which has degree one less than the original dividend
    [ ] The remainder of the division
    [ ] A polynomial that cannot be factored
    [ ] A polynomial with negative leading coefficient
    Explanation: The depressed polynomial is the quotient from synthetic division. Dividing by a linear factor reduces the degree by 1, so a degree-n polynomial yields a degree-(n−1) quotient.

  Q18. What is 'polynomial long division'?
    [*] A general method for dividing any polynomial by another, similar to long division with numbers
    [ ] A way to factor polynomials
    [ ] A method for multiplying polynomials
    [ ] A shortcut that only works for linear divisors
    Explanation: Polynomial long division works for any polynomial divisor. The steps are: divide the leading terms, multiply, subtract, bring down, and repeat.

  Q19. What does the 'division algorithm for polynomials' state?
    [ ] The remainder is always zero under normal circumstances
    [ ] f(x) = d(x) · r(x), particularly regarding does based on established principles
    [ ] f(x) = d(x) + q(x) + r(x) within the typical range of values
    [*] f(x) = d(x) · q(x) + r(x), where the degree of r(x) is less than the degree of d(x)
    Explanation: The division algorithm states: Dividend = Divisor times Quotient + Remainder, where the degree of the remainder is strictly less than the degree of the divisor.

  Q20. What does 'evaluate a polynomial using synthetic substitution' mean?
    [*] Using synthetic division to find f(c) by reading the remainder when dividing by (x − c)
    [ ] Finding the degree of the polynomial at a point
    [ ] Plugging in a value directly into each term
    [ ] Graphing the polynomial to estimate values
    Explanation: Synthetic substitution uses the synthetic division process to evaluate f(c). By the Remainder Theorem, the final number in the synthetic division process equals f(c).

  Q21. An engineer tests if a load value x = 2 is a critical point for stress function f(x) = x³ − 4x² + x + 6. Using synthetic division, what is f(2)?
    [ ] −4
    [ ] 2
    [ ] 6
    [*] 0
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 2 and [1, −4, 1, 6]: 1; 2+(−4)=−2; −4+1=−3; −6+6=0. Remainder = 0, so f(2) = 0 and x = 2 is a critical point.

  Q22. A pharmaceutical company models drug concentration as C(t) = 2t³ − 5t² − 4t + 3. Use synthetic division to evaluate C(3) and determine if the drug is fully eliminated at t = 3.
    [ ] C(3) = 3
    [ ] C(3) = −12, so the concentration is negative
    [ ] C(3) = 6, so the drug is not eliminated
    [*] C(3) = 0, so the drug is fully eliminated at t = 3
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 3 and [2, −5, −4, 3]: 2; 6+(−5)=1; 3+(−4)=−1; −3+3=0. C(3) = 0, confirming full elimination.

  Q23. A cost function is C(x) = x³ + 3x² − 10x − 24. A manager suspects (x + 2) is a factor. Is this correct?
    [ ] Cannot be determined
    [ ] No, the remainder is −24
    [*] Yes, synthetic division gives remainder 0
    [ ] No, the remainder is 4
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = −2 and [1, 3, −10, −24]: 1; −2+3=1; −2+(−10)=−12; 24+(−24)=0. Remainder = 0 confirms (x + 2) is a factor.

  Q24. A student divides f(x) = x⁴ + 3x³ − x − 3 by (x + 3). What is the depressed (quotient) polynomial?
    [ ] x³ + 3x² − 1
    [*] x³ − 1
    [ ] x³ + 1
    [ ] x³ + 6x² − 1
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = −3 and [1, 3, 0, −1, −3]: 1; −3+3=0; 0+0=0; 0+(−1)=−1; 3+(−3)=0. Quotient: x³ − 1, remainder 0.

  Q25. A data analyst models revenue as R(x) = 4x³ − 8x² + x − 2. She divides by (x − 2). What are the quotient and remainder?
    [ ] Quotient: 4x² − 1; Remainder: 0
    [ ] Quotient: 4x² + 1; Remainder: 2
    [*] Quotient: 4x² + 1; Remainder: 0
    [ ] Quotient: 4x² − 8x + 1; Remainder: −2
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 2 and [4, −8, 1, −2]: 4; 8+(−8)=0; 0+1=1; 2+(−2)=0. Quotient: 4x² + 1, remainder 0.

  Q26. A bridge load function is f(x) = x³ − 6x² + 11x − 6. After dividing by (x − 1), what is the quotient that reveals the remaining factors?
    [ ] x² − 7x + 6
    [ ] x² − 6x + 11
    [ ] x² − 4x + 3 = (x − 1)(x − 3)
    [*] x² − 5x + 6 = (x − 2)(x − 3)
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 1 and [1, −6, 11, −6]: 1; 1+(−6)=−5; −5+11=6; 6+(−6)=0. Quotient: x² − 5x + 6 = (x − 2)(x − 3).

  Q27. A shipping cost function is C(x) = x³ − 2x² − 5x + 6. Using x = 1 as a known root, factor C(x) completely.
    [ ] (x − 1)(x + 3)(x − 2)
    [*] (x − 1)(x − 3)(x + 2)
    [ ] (x − 1)(x² − x − 6)
    [ ] (x − 1)(x − 2)(x − 3)
    Explanation: Divide by (x − 1): quotient x² − x − 6 = (x − 3)(x + 2). So C(x) = (x − 1)(x − 3)(x + 2).

  Q28. A population model is P(t) = t³ + 6t² + 11t + 6. Using t = −1 as a root, factor P(t) completely.
    [ ] (t − 1)(t + 2)(t + 3)
    [ ] (t + 1)(t + 6)(t + 1)
    [*] (t + 1)(t + 2)(t + 3)
    [ ] (t + 1)(t² + 5t + 6)
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = −1 and [1, 6, 11, 6]: 1; −1+6=5; −5+11=6; −6+6=0. Quotient: t² + 5t + 6 = (t + 2)(t + 3). Full: (t + 1)(t + 2)(t + 3).

  Q29. A trade balance model is f(x) = 2x³ + x² − 13x + 6. Using synthetic division, verify that x = 2 is a root and factor completely.
    [ ] (x − 2)(2x² + 5x − 3)
    [ ] (x + 2)(2x − 1)(x − 3)
    [ ] (x − 2)(2x + 1)(x − 3)
    [*] (x − 2)(2x − 1)(x + 3)
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 2 and [2, 1, −13, 6]: 2; 4+1=5; 10+(−13)=−3; −6+6=0. Quotient: 2x² + 5x − 3 = (2x − 1)(x + 3). Full: (x − 2)(2x − 1)(x + 3).

  Q30. A chemist's reaction rate is modeled by R(t) = t⁴ − 5t³ + 5t² + 5t − 6. She knows t = 1 and t = 2 are roots. Using successive synthetic division, what is the remaining quadratic factor?
    [ ] t² − 5t + 6
    [ ] t² + 2t − 3
    [ ] t² − 3t + 2
    [*] t² − 2t − 3 = (t − 3)(t + 1)
    Explanation: Divide by (t − 1): [1, −5, 5, 5, −6] gives quotient t³ − 4t² + t + 6. Divide by (t − 2): [1, −4, 1, 6] gives quotient t² − 2t − 3 = (t − 3)(t + 1). Full: (t − 1)(t − 2)(t − 3)(t + 1).

--- Lesson 3.3: Synthetic Division (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Divide x³ - 6x² + 11x - 6 by x - 1
     A: x² - 5x + 6. Quotient: use long or synthetic division.

  2. Q: Synthetic division: (2x³ + 3x - 5) ÷ (x - 2)
     A: Quotient 2x² + 4x + 11, remainder 17.

  3. Q: Use remainder theorem: f(x) = x³ - 2x + 5, remainder when divided by (x + 1)?
     A: f(-1) = -1 + 2 + 5 = 6. Remainder is 6.

  4. Q: Divide x⁴ - 16 by x - 2
     A: Quotient x³ + 2x² + 4x + 8, remainder 0 (exact division).

  5. Q: Is (x - 3) a factor of x³ - 4x² + x + 6?
     A: Test: f(3) = 27 - 36 + 3 + 6 = 0. Yes, it's a factor.

  6. Q: Quotient when x⁴ + 2x³ - x² - 4x is divided by x + 2?
     A: x³ - x + 2. Use synthetic division or factor x(x³+2x²-x-4).

  7. Q: Remainder of (3x³ - 5x + 2) ÷ (x + 1)?
     A: f(-1) = -3 + 5 + 2 = 4. Remainder is 4.

  8. Q: Setup for synthetic division: divisor (x - 5)
     A: Use 5 in synthetic division. For (x - c), use c in box.

  9. Q: If f(x) = (x - 2)q(x) + r, what is r?
     A: r = f(2). Remainder when f(x) divided by (x - 2).

  10. Q: Write P(x) = (x-1)Q(x) + 5 where Q(x) is quotient
     A: Form shows P(x) divided by (x-1) has quotient Q(x) and remainder 5.

--- Lesson 3.4: Polynomial Graphs & Zeros (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Polynomial Graphs & Zeros
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the end behavior of f(x) = −2x⁴ + 5x²?
The leading term is −2x⁴. Even degree means both ends go the same direction, and the negative leading coefficient means both ends go to −∞.
What is the end behavior of f(x) = 3x⁵ − x² + 7?
The leading term is 3x⁵. Odd degree with a positive leading coefficient means the graph rises to the right and falls to the left.
For f(x) = (x + 1)²(x − 2), what is the multiplicity of the zero at x = −1?
The factor (x + 1) is raised to the second power, so the zero at x = −1 has multiplicity 2. The graph touches but does not cross the x-axis at this point.
What is the maximum number of turning points for a degree-5 polynomial?
A polynomial of degree n has at most n − 1 turning points. For a degree-5 polynomial, the maximum number of turning points is 5 − 1 = 4.
What is the y-intercept of f(x) = 2x³ − 5x² + 3x − 7?
The y-intercept is found by evaluating f(0). Substituting x = 0 gives f(0) = 0 − 0 + 0 − 7 = −7, so the y-intercept is (0, −7).
What is a 'zero' (or 'root') of a polynomial function?
A zero (or root) of a polynomial is an x-value that makes the function equal to zero. Graphically, zeros correspond to the x-intercepts where the graph meets the x-axis.
What is a 'turning point' of a polynomial graph?
A turning point is a local maximum or local minimum on the graph where the function switches direction, changing from increasing to decreasing or from decreasing to increasing.
What is the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra?
The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra states that every polynomial of degree n >= 1 with complex coefficients has at least one complex root, and consequently exactly n roots when counting multiplicity.
What is the 'degree' of a polynomial and how does it relate to the number of zeros?
The degree of a polynomial is the highest power of x. A polynomial of degree n can have at most n real zeros and has exactly n zeros over the complex numbers (counting multiplicity).
A roller coaster's height in feet is modeled by h(x) = −0.5x⁴ + 8x³ − 40x² + 64x for the first section, where x is the horizontal distance in hundreds of feet. What is the end behavior of this function as x → +∞?
The leading term is −0.5x⁴. Since the degree is even (4) and the leading coefficient is negative (−0.5), both ends of the graph go to −∞, meaning h(x) → −∞ as x → +∞.
Real-World Applications
Example: At a zero with even multiplicity, the graph of a polynomial:
When a zero has even multiplicity (2, 4, 6, ...), the graph touches the x-axis at that point but does not cross it, bouncing back in the direction it came from.
Example: For f(x) = (x + 1)²(x − 2), what is the multiplicity of the zero at x = −1?
The factor (x + 1) is raised to the second power, so the zero at x = −1 has multiplicity 2. The graph touches but does not cross the x-axis at this point.
Example: At a zero with odd multiplicity, the graph of a polynomial:
When a zero has odd multiplicity (1, 3, 5, ...), the graph crosses through the x-axis at that point, changing from positive to negative or vice versa.
Example: What does 'multiplicity' of a zero mean?
Multiplicity refers to how many times a particular linear factor appears in the factored form of a polynomial. For example, in (x − 2)³, the zero x = 2 has multiplicity 3.
Example: What does it mean for a polynomial to have a zero of multiplicity 2 at x = 4?
A zero of multiplicity 2 at x = 4 means the factor (x − 4) appears twice in the factored form of the polynomial, i.e., (x − 4)² is a factor. The graph touches the x-axis at x = 4 without crossing.

--- Lesson 3.4: Polynomial Graphs & Zeros (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What are the zeros of f(x) = (x − 1)(x + 2)(x − 3)?
    [ ] x = −1, 2, −3
    [ ] x = 0, 1, −2
    [ ] x = 1, 2, 3
    [*] x = 1, −2, 3
    Explanation: Set each factor equal to zero: x − 1 = 0 gives x = 1, x + 2 = 0 gives x = −2, and x − 3 = 0 gives x = 3.

  Q2. What is the end behavior of f(x) = −2x⁴ + 5x²?
    [ ] Left goes to +∞, right goes to −∞
    [*] Both ends go to −∞
    [ ] Left goes to −∞, right goes to +∞
    [ ] Both ends go to +∞
    Explanation: The leading term is −2x⁴. Even degree means both ends go the same direction, and the negative leading coefficient means both ends go to −∞.

  Q3. At a zero with even multiplicity, the graph of a polynomial:
    [ ] Crosses the x-axis
    [ ] Has a hole
    [*] Touches the x-axis and turns around
    [ ] Has a vertical asymptote
    Explanation: When a zero has even multiplicity (2, 4, 6, ...), the graph touches the x-axis at that point but does not cross it, bouncing back in the direction it came from.

  Q4. What are the zeros of f(x) = x³ − 2x² − 8x?
    [ ] x = 1, −2, 4
    [ ] x = −1, 2, −4
    [*] x = 0, 4, −2
    [ ] x = 0, 2, 4
    Explanation: Factor out x first: x(x² − 2x − 8) = x(x − 4)(x + 2). Setting each factor to zero gives x = 0, x = 4, and x = −2.

  Q5. What is the end behavior of f(x) = 3x⁵ − x² + 7?
    [*] As x → +∞, f(x) → +∞; as x → −∞, f(x) → −∞
    [ ] As x → +∞, f(x) → −∞; as x → −∞, f(x) → +∞
    [ ] Both ends go to −∞
    [ ] Both ends go to +∞
    Explanation: The leading term is 3x⁵. Odd degree with a positive leading coefficient means the graph rises to the right and falls to the left.

  Q6. For f(x) = (x + 1)²(x − 2), what is the multiplicity of the zero at x = −1?
    [ ] 0
    [ ] 1
    [*] 2
    [ ] 3
    Explanation: The factor (x + 1) is raised to the second power, so the zero at x = −1 has multiplicity 2. The graph touches but does not cross the x-axis at this point.

  Q7. What is the maximum number of turning points for a degree-5 polynomial?
    [*] 4
    [ ] 3
    [ ] 6
    [ ] 5
    Explanation: A polynomial of degree n has at most n − 1 turning points. For a degree-5 polynomial, the maximum number of turning points is 5 − 1 = 4.

  Q8. What is the y-intercept of f(x) = 2x³ − 5x² + 3x − 7?
    [ ] (0, 7)
    [*] (0, −7)
    [ ] (0, 3)
    [ ] (0, 2)
    Explanation: The y-intercept is found by evaluating f(0). Substituting x = 0 gives f(0) = 0 − 0 + 0 − 7 = −7, so the y-intercept is (0, −7).

  Q9. How many x-intercepts can f(x) = x⁴ − 3x² + 2 have at most?
    [ ] 2
    [ ] 5
    [ ] 3
    [*] 4
    Explanation: A polynomial of degree n can have at most n real zeros (x-intercepts). Since this is a degree-4 polynomial, it can have at most 4 x-intercepts.

  Q10. At a zero with odd multiplicity, the graph of a polynomial:
    [ ] Touches and bounces off the x-axis
    [*] Crosses the x-axis
    [ ] Skips the x-axis entirely
    [ ] Has a vertical asymptote
    Explanation: When a zero has odd multiplicity (1, 3, 5, ...), the graph crosses through the x-axis at that point, changing from positive to negative or vice versa.

  Q11. What is a 'zero' (or 'root') of a polynomial function?
    [*] A value of x where f(x) = 0
    [ ] The degree of the polynomial
    [ ] The y-intercept of the graph
    [ ] The leading coefficient
    Explanation: A zero (or root) of a polynomial is an x-value that makes the function equal to zero. Graphically, zeros correspond to the x-intercepts where the graph meets the x-axis.

  Q12. What does 'multiplicity' of a zero mean?
    [ ] The degree of the polynomial
    [*] The number of times a factor is repeated in the factored form
    [ ] The value of the zero itself
    [ ] The number of zeros the polynomial has
    Explanation: Multiplicity refers to how many times a particular linear factor appears in the factored form of a polynomial. For example, in (x − 2)³, the zero x = 2 has multiplicity 3.

  Q13. What does the term 'end behavior' describe for a polynomial function?
    [ ] The value of the polynomial at x = 0
    [*] What happens to f(x) as x approaches positive or negative infinity
    [ ] Where the polynomial starts being defined
    [ ] The maximum value of the polynomial
    Explanation: End behavior describes the long-run trend of a polynomial function, specifically how the output values behave as the input values become very large (positive or negative).

  Q14. What is a 'turning point' of a polynomial graph?
    [ ] A point where the graph is undefined
    [*] A point where the graph changes from increasing to decreasing or vice versa
    [ ] A point where the graph crosses the x-axis
    [ ] The y-intercept of the graph
    Explanation: A turning point is a local maximum or local minimum on the graph where the function switches direction, changing from increasing to decreasing or from decreasing to increasing.

  Q15. What does the Intermediate Value Theorem guarantee for continuous functions?
    [ ] Every polynomial has at least two real zeros
    [*] If f(a) and f(b) have opposite signs, there is at least one zero between a and b
    [ ] The function has a maximum between a and b
    [ ] The function is always increasing between a and b
    Explanation: The Intermediate Value Theorem states that if a continuous function has values of opposite signs at two points, it must cross zero somewhere between those points, guaranteeing at least one real root in that interval.

  Q16. What does 'leading coefficient' mean in a polynomial?
    [ ] The constant term
    [ ] The first coefficient written in the expression
    [ ] The largest coefficient in the polynomial
    [*] The coefficient of the term with the highest degree
    Explanation: The leading coefficient is the coefficient attached to the highest-degree term of a polynomial. It determines the end behavior of the graph along with the degree.

  Q17. What does it mean for a polynomial to have a zero of multiplicity 2 at x = 4?
    [*] (x − 4)² is a factor of the polynomial
    [ ] The polynomial equals 2 when x = 4
    [ ] The polynomial has two different zeros at x = 4 and x = −4
    [ ] The graph crosses the x-axis twice at x = 4
    Explanation: A zero of multiplicity 2 at x = 4 means the factor (x − 4) appears twice in the factored form of the polynomial, i.e., (x − 4)² is a factor. The graph touches the x-axis at x = 4 without crossing.

  Q18. What is the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra?
    [ ] All polynomials can be factored completely over the integers
    [*] Every non-constant polynomial has at least one complex root
    [ ] The degree of a polynomial equals its number of turning points
    [ ] Every polynomial has only real roots
    Explanation: The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra states that every polynomial of degree n >= 1 with complex coefficients has at least one complex root, and consequently exactly n roots when counting multiplicity.

  Q19. What is the 'degree' of a polynomial and how does it relate to the number of zeros?
    [ ] The number of terms; it equals the number of zeros
    [ ] The largest coefficient; it determines the sign of zeros
    [*] The highest exponent; a degree-n polynomial has at most n real zeros
    [ ] The constant term; it has no relation to zeros
    Explanation: The degree of a polynomial is the highest power of x. A polynomial of degree n can have at most n real zeros and has exactly n zeros over the complex numbers (counting multiplicity).

  Q20. What does an 'x-intercept' represent on the graph of a polynomial?
    [ ] The highest point on the graph
    [ ] A point where the graph crosses the y-axis
    [ ] A point where the graph has a vertical tangent line
    [*] A point where the graph crosses or touches the x-axis, where f(x) = 0
    Explanation: An x-intercept is a point on the graph where y = 0, meaning the graph meets the x-axis. Each x-intercept corresponds to a real zero of the polynomial.

  Q21. A civil engineer models the cross-section profile of a hill using f(x) = −x³ + 6x² − 9x, where x is the horizontal distance in hundreds of meters and f(x) is the elevation in meters. What are the x-values where the elevation is at ground level?
    [ ] x = 3 only
    [*] x = 0 and x = 3 (multiplicity 2)
    [ ] x = 0, x = 1, and x = 9
    [ ] x = 0, x = −3, and x = 3
    Explanation: Set f(x) = 0: −x³ + 6x² − 9x = −x(x² − 6x + 9) = −x(x − 3)². The zeros are x = 0 (multiplicity 1) and x = 3 (multiplicity 2), meaning the profile touches the ground at 300 meters but doesn't cross.

  Q22. A roller coaster's height in feet is modeled by h(x) = −0.5x⁴ + 8x³ − 40x² + 64x for the first section, where x is the horizontal distance in hundreds of feet. What is the end behavior of this function as x → +∞?
    [ ] h(x) → +∞
    [ ] h(x) → 0
    [*] h(x) → −∞
    [ ] h(x) → 64
    Explanation: The leading term is −0.5x⁴. Since the degree is even (4) and the leading coefficient is negative (−0.5), both ends of the graph go to −∞, meaning h(x) → −∞ as x → +∞.

  Q23. A population biologist models a species count (in thousands) over time with P(t) = t³ − 12t² + 36t, where t is years after observation began. At what times does the population return to zero?
    [ ] t = 6 only
    [*] t = 0 and t = 6 (multiplicity 2)
    [ ] t = 0, t = 4, and t = 9
    [ ] t = 0, t = 3, and t = 12
    Explanation: Factor: P(t) = t(t² − 12t + 36) = t(t − 6)². The zeros are t = 0 and t = 6 (with multiplicity 2). At t = 6, the population touches zero but turns back, suggesting a temporary decline.

  Q24. A student is sketching the graph of f(x) = (x + 3)(x − 1)²(x − 4). How many turning points should the student expect to see at most?
    [*] 3
    [ ] 1
    [ ] 2
    [ ] 4
    Explanation: The degree of f(x) = (x + 3)(x − 1)²(x − 4) is 1 + 2 + 1 = 4. A degree-4 polynomial has at most n − 1 = 3 turning points.

  Q25. A company's monthly profit in thousands of dollars is modeled by P(x) = −x⁴ + 10x³ − 35x² + 50x − 24, where x represents a production scaling factor. If P(1) = 0, P(2) = 0, P(3) = 0, and P(4) = 0, how does the graph behave at each x-intercept?
    [ ] The graph crosses at x = 1 and x = 4, touches at x = 2 and x = 3
    [ ] The graph touches and bounces at all four points
    [*] The graph crosses the x-axis at all four points since each zero has multiplicity 1
    [ ] Cannot be determined without more information
    Explanation: Since the polynomial is degree 4 and has 4 distinct real zeros (x = 1, 2, 3, 4), each zero must have multiplicity 1. Odd multiplicity means the graph crosses the x-axis at each intercept.

  Q26. An architect models the curved roofline of a building with h(x) = −(x − 2)²(x − 8)², where x is in meters and h(x) is height in meters. At which x-values does the roofline touch the ground, and what happens at those points?
    [ ] x = 2 and x = 8; the graph crosses the x-axis at both
    [ ] x = 2 and x = 8; the graph crosses at x = 2 and touches at x = 8
    [ ] x = −2 and x = −8; the graph touches at both
    [*] x = 2 and x = 8; the graph touches but does not cross the x-axis at both
    Explanation: Both zeros x = 2 and x = 8 have multiplicity 2 (even), so the graph touches the x-axis without crossing at both points. The roofline meets the ground at x = 2 m and x = 8 m.

  Q27. A marine biologist records water temperature changes (in degrees above baseline) with T(t) = t⁵ − 5t³ + 4t, where t is months since January. How many times does the temperature equal the baseline?
    [ ] 4 times
    [*] 5 times
    [ ] 2 times
    [ ] 3 times
    Explanation: Factor: T(t) = t(t⁴ − 5t² + 4) = t(t² − 1)(t² − 4) = t(t − 1)(t + 1)(t − 2)(t + 2). There are 5 distinct real zeros: t = 0, ±1, ±2, so the temperature equals baseline 5 times.

  Q28. A physics teacher gives students the function f(x) = 2x³ + 3x² − 5x − 6 and asks them to verify that there is a root between x = 1 and x = 2 using the Intermediate Value Theorem. What values of f(1) and f(2) confirm this?
    [*] f(1) = −6 and f(2) = 12, opposite signs confirm a root exists
    [ ] f(1) = −2 and f(2) = −4, both negative so no root exists
    [ ] f(1) = 6 and f(2) = 12, both positive so no root exists
    [ ] f(1) = 0 and f(2) = 0, both are roots
    Explanation: Calculate f(1) = 2 + 3 − 5 − 6 = −6 and f(2) = 16 + 12 − 10 − 6 = 12. Since f(1) < 0 and f(2) > 0, the signs differ, so the IVT guarantees at least one root between x = 1 and x = 2.

  Q29. A landscaper designs a garden border whose shape follows f(x) = x⁴ − 8x² + 16. She notices the curve touches the x-axis at two points but never crosses it. What are those x-values?
    [*] x = −2 and x = 2, each with multiplicity 2
    [ ] x = 0 with multiplicity 4
    [ ] x = −4 and x = 4, each with multiplicity 1
    [ ] x = −2 and x = 2, each with multiplicity 1
    Explanation: Factor: x⁴ − 8x² + 16 = (x² − 4)² = [(x − 2)(x + 2)]² = (x − 2)²(x + 2)². The zeros are x = 2 and x = −2, each with multiplicity 2. Even multiplicity means the graph touches but does not cross the x-axis.

  Q30. A data analyst models website traffic (in thousands of visitors) with V(t) = −2t³ + 18t² − 48t + 32, where t is months since a product launch. If V(1) = 0, the analyst factors out (t − 1). What are all the zeros of V(t)?
    [*] t = 1 and t = 4 (multiplicity 2)
    [ ] t = 1, t = 2, and t = 8
    [ ] t = 2 (multiplicity 2) and t = 4
    [ ] t = 1, t = 2, and t = 4
    Explanation: V(1) = −2 + 18 − 48 + 32 = 0, confirming (t − 1) is a factor. Synthetic division gives quotient −2t² + 16t − 32 = −2(t² − 8t + 16) = −2(t − 4)². So V(t) = −2(t − 1)(t − 4)² with zeros at t = 1 and t = 4 (multiplicity 2).

--- Lesson 3.4: Polynomial Graphs & Zeros (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Find zeros of f(x) = (x - 1)(x + 2)²(x - 3)
     A: x = 1, -2 (multiplicity 2), 3. Factors give zeros.

  2. Q: End behavior of f(x) = -2x⁴ + 5x²
     A: As x→∞, f→-∞; as x→-∞, f→-∞. Even degree, negative leading coeff.

  3. Q: Multiplicity 3 zero at x = 2 means what?
     A: Graph crosses at x=2 with inflection point. Odd multiplicity = crosses.

  4. Q: Find all zeros: f(x) = x³ - 2x² - 8x
     A: x = 0, 4, -2. Factor: x(x² - 2x - 8) = x(x-4)(x+2).

  5. Q: Even multiplicity zero behavior
     A: Graph touches but doesn't cross x-axis (bounce). Local extremum.

  6. Q: Sketch f(x) = (x + 1)²(x - 2). Zeros, multiplicities, end behavior?
     A: Zeros: -1 (mult. 2, touches), 2 (mult. 1, crosses). Opens up (positive leading coeff, degree 3).

  7. Q: Number of real zeros: degree 5 polynomial
     A: Can have 1, 3, or 5 real zeros. Complex zeros come in pairs.

  8. Q: Local maximum/minimum occur at zeros of what?
     A: Zeros of derivative f'(x). Critical points.

  9. Q: If zero has multiplicity 4, touching or crossing?
     A: Touching (even multiplicity). Local extremum, doesn't cross.

  10. Q: Write polynomial: zeros at -3, 0, 2; passes (1, 4)
     A: f(x) = a(x+3)x(x-2). Find a: f(1) = a(4)(1)(-1) = -4a = 4, so a = -1.

--- Lesson 3.5: Remainder & Factor Theorems (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Remainder & Factor Theorems
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
Using the Remainder Theorem, what is the remainder when f(x) = 2x³ + 5x − 3 is divided by (x − 2)?
By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(2) = 2(8) + 5(2) − 3 = 16 + 10 − 3 = 23.
What is the remainder when f(x) = 3x⁵ − 2x² + 7 is divided by (x + 2)?
Dividing by (x + 2) means c = −2. Compute f(−2) = 3(−32) − 2(4) + 7 = −96 − 8 + 7 = −97.
What is the remainder when f(x) = x⁴ − 1 is divided by (x − 1)?
By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(1) = 1⁴ − 1 = 0. This also tells us (x − 1) is a factor of x⁴ − 1.
What is the remainder when f(x) = 4x² − 3x + 5 is divided by (x − 1)?
By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(1) = 4(1)² − 3(1) + 5 = 4 − 3 + 5 = 6.
What is a 'depressed polynomial' in the context of polynomial division?
A depressed polynomial is the quotient obtained when dividing a polynomial by one of its linear factors. Its degree is one less than the original, and it can be further analyzed to find remaining roots.
What is meant by 'reducing the degree' of a polynomial when finding roots?
Once a root r is found, we factor out (x − r) using synthetic division to obtain a quotient polynomial of one lower degree. We then apply root-finding methods to this simpler polynomial to find remaining roots.
What is the relationship between the Remainder Theorem and the Factor Theorem?
The Factor Theorem is a direct consequence of the Remainder Theorem. Since the remainder when dividing by (x − c) equals f(c), a remainder of zero (i.e., f(c) = 0) means (x − c) divides evenly, making it a factor.
A pharmacist models a drug concentration in mg/L as C(t) = t³ − 6t² + 11t − 6, where t is hours after administration. She tests t = 1 and finds C(1) = 0. After dividing out (t − 1), what is the depressed polynomial?
Synthetic division of t³ − 6t² + 11t − 6 by (t − 1): bring down 1; 1·1 = 1, −6+1 = −5; 1·(−5) = −5, 11+(−5) = 6; 1·6 = 6, −6+6 = 0. The depressed polynomial is t² − 5t + 6 = (t − 2)(t − 3).
A city planner models a drainage function as D(x) = 2x⁴ − 5x³ − 4x² + 15x − 6, where x is a flow rate parameter. If x = 1/2 is a root, what is the depressed polynomial after dividing by (x − 1/2)?
Synthetic division with c = 1/2 on coefficients 2, −5, −4, 15, −6: bring down 2; (1/2)(2) = 1, −5+1 = −4; (1/2)(−4) = −2, −4+(−2) = −6; (1/2)(−6) = −3, 15+(−3) = 12; (1/2)(12) = 6, −6+6 = 0. Quotient: 2x³ − 4x² − 6x + 12.
Real-World Applications
Example: A city planner models a drainage function as D(x) = 2x⁴ − 5x³ − 4x² + 15x − 6, where x is a flow rate parameter. If x = 1/2 is a root, what is the depressed polynomial after dividing by (x − 1/2)?
Synthetic division with c = 1/2 on coefficients 2, −5, −4, 15, −6: bring down 2; (1/2)(2) = 1, −5+1 = −4; (1/2)(−4) = −2, −4+(−2) = −6; (1/2)(−6) = −3, 15+(−3) = 12; (1/2)(12) = 6, −6+6 = 0. Quotient: 2x³ − 4x² − 6x + 12.

--- Lesson 3.5: Remainder & Factor Theorems (Quiz) ---

  Q1. Using the Remainder Theorem, what is the remainder when f(x) = 2x³ + 5x − 3 is divided by (x − 2)?
    [ ] 25
    [ ] 19
    [ ] 21
    [*] 23
    Explanation: By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(2) = 2(8) + 5(2) − 3 = 16 + 10 − 3 = 23.

  Q2. Is (x + 1) a factor of f(x) = x³ + 2x² + x?
    [ ] Yes, because f(1) = 0
    [ ] Cannot be determined
    [ ] No, because f(−1) ≠ 0
    [*] Yes, because f(−1) = 0
    Explanation: By the Factor Theorem, (x + 1) is a factor if f(−1) = 0. Calculate: f(−1) = (−1)³ + 2(−1)² + (−1) = −1 + 2 − 1 = 0. Since f(−1) = 0, (x + 1) is a factor.

  Q3. What is the remainder when f(x) = 3x⁵ − 2x² + 7 is divided by (x + 2)?
    [*] −97
    [ ] −89
    [ ] 89
    [ ] 97
    Explanation: Dividing by (x + 2) means c = −2. Compute f(−2) = 3(−32) − 2(4) + 7 = −96 − 8 + 7 = −97.

  Q4. If f(3) = 0 for a polynomial f(x), which of the following must be a factor of f(x)?
    [*] (x − 3)
    [ ] (3x − 1)
    [ ] (x + 3)
    [ ] (x − 1)
    Explanation: By the Factor Theorem, if f(c) = 0 then (x − c) is a factor of f(x). Since f(3) = 0, (x − 3) is a factor.

  Q5. Using the Remainder Theorem, find f(2) for f(x) = x³ − 4x + 1.
    [ ] −5
    [ ] 3
    [ ] 5
    [*] 1
    Explanation: Evaluate f(2) = (2)³ − 4(2) + 1 = 8 − 8 + 1 = 1. By the Remainder Theorem, this is also the remainder when f(x) is divided by (x − 2).

  Q6. Is (x − 2) a factor of f(x) = x³ − 3x² + 4?
    [ ] Yes, because f(−2) = 0
    [ ] No, because f(2) = −2
    [*] Yes, because f(2) = 0
    [ ] No, because f(2) = 4
    Explanation: Check f(2) = 8 − 12 + 4 = 0. Since f(2) = 0, the Factor Theorem tells us (x − 2) is a factor of f(x).

  Q7. What are the possible rational roots of f(x) = 2x³ − x² − 5x + 3?
    [ ] ±1/2, ±3/2 only
    [ ] ±1, ±2, ±3
    [*] ±1, ±3, ±1/2, ±3/2
    [ ] ±1, ±3 only
    Explanation: By the Rational Root Theorem, possible roots are ±(factors of constant)/(factors of leading coefficient) = ±(factors of 3)/(factors of 2) = ±1, ±3, ±1/2, ±3/2.

  Q8. What is the remainder when f(x) = x⁴ − 1 is divided by (x − 1)?
    [ ] 2
    [*] 0
    [ ] −1
    [ ] 1
    Explanation: By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(1) = 1⁴ − 1 = 0. This also tells us (x − 1) is a factor of x⁴ − 1.

  Q9. When you divide f(x) by (x − 2) and get a remainder of 0, what does this tell you?
    [ ] f(x) has no real zeros
    [ ] x = −2 is a root of f(x)
    [ ] The quotient is zero
    [*] (x − 2) is a factor of f(x)
    Explanation: A remainder of 0 when dividing by (x − 2) means f(2) = 0, which by the Factor Theorem confirms that (x − 2) is a factor of f(x) and x = 2 is a root.

  Q10. What is the remainder when f(x) = 4x² − 3x + 5 is divided by (x − 1)?
    [*] 6
    [ ] 5
    [ ] 4
    [ ] 2
    Explanation: By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is f(1) = 4(1)² − 3(1) + 5 = 4 − 3 + 5 = 6.

  Q11. What does the Remainder Theorem state?
    [ ] The remainder is always zero for any polynomial division
    [ ] The remainder equals the leading coefficient times c
    [ ] When f(x) is divided by (x − c), the remainder equals f(0)
    [*] When f(x) is divided by (x − c), the remainder equals f(c)
    Explanation: The Remainder Theorem states that when a polynomial f(x) is divided by a linear divisor (x − c), the remainder is the value f(c). This provides a quick way to evaluate polynomials.

  Q12. What does the Factor Theorem state?
    [ ] (x − c) is a factor only when c is a positive integer
    [ ] Every polynomial can be factored into linear factors over the reals
    [ ] (x − c) is a factor of f(x) if f(c) = 1
    [*] (x − c) is a factor of f(x) if and only if f(c) = 0
    Explanation: The Factor Theorem is a direct consequence of the Remainder Theorem: (x − c) is a factor of f(x) if and only if f(c) = 0, meaning the remainder upon division is zero.

  Q13. What does the Rational Root Theorem tell us about a polynomial with integer coefficients?
    [*] Any rational root p/q has p dividing the constant term and q dividing the leading coefficient
    [ ] Every polynomial with integer coefficients has at least one rational root
    [ ] The number of rational roots equals the degree
    [ ] The rational roots are always integers
    Explanation: The Rational Root Theorem states that for a polynomial with integer coefficients, any rational root in lowest terms p/q must have p as a factor of the constant term and q as a factor of the leading coefficient.

  Q14. What is a 'depressed polynomial' in the context of polynomial division?
    [ ] A polynomial that has no real roots
    [*] The quotient polynomial obtained after dividing out a factor, which has one lower degree
    [ ] The remainder after division
    [ ] A polynomial with all negative coefficients
    Explanation: A depressed polynomial is the quotient obtained when dividing a polynomial by one of its linear factors. Its degree is one less than the original, and it can be further analyzed to find remaining roots.

  Q15. What does 'synthetic substitution' mean in polynomial evaluation?
    [ ] Graphing the polynomial to estimate values
    [ ] Replacing a polynomial with a simpler expression
    [ ] Substituting imaginary numbers for real ones
    [*] Using synthetic division to evaluate f(c) by reading the remainder
    Explanation: Synthetic substitution is the process of using synthetic division to evaluate a polynomial at a specific value. The remainder obtained is the function value f(c), which is often faster than direct substitution for high-degree polynomials.

  Q16. What does the polynomial Division Algorithm state?
    [ ] The remainder must always be a polynomial of the same degree
    [ ] Polynomial division only works with integer coefficients
    [ ] Every polynomial can be divided evenly by any linear factor
    [*] f(x) = d(x) · q(x) + r, where d is the divisor, q is the quotient, and r is the remainder
    Explanation: The Division Algorithm states that for any polynomial f(x) and nonzero divisor d(x), there exist unique polynomials q(x) (quotient) and r(x) (remainder) such that f(x) = d(x) · q(x) + r(x), where the degree of r is less than the degree of d.

  Q17. What does it mean for a polynomial to have 'no rational roots'?
    [ ] The polynomial cannot be factored in any way
    [*] None of the candidates from the Rational Root Theorem make f(x) = 0; roots may be irrational or complex
    [ ] The polynomial has only negative roots
    [ ] The polynomial has no roots at all
    Explanation: When no rational root candidates produce a zero, the polynomial may still have irrational roots (like square roots) or complex roots. The Rational Root Theorem only tests for rational possibilities.

  Q18. What is meant by 'reducing the degree' of a polynomial when finding roots?
    [ ] Converting a polynomial into a linear equation
    [ ] Removing the highest-degree term from the polynomial
    [ ] Lowering the exponents of every term by 1
    [*] After finding one root r, dividing by (x − r) to get a lower-degree polynomial for finding remaining roots
