    Explanation: Once a root r is found, we factor out (x − r) using synthetic division to obtain a quotient polynomial of one lower degree. We then apply root-finding methods to this simpler polynomial to find remaining roots.

  Q19. What is the relationship between the Remainder Theorem and the Factor Theorem?
    [ ] The Remainder Theorem only applies to quadratic polynomials
    [ ] They are completely unrelated theorems
    [ ] The Factor Theorem applies only to cubic polynomials
    [*] The Factor Theorem is a special case of the Remainder Theorem where the remainder is zero
    Explanation: The Factor Theorem is a direct consequence of the Remainder Theorem. Since the remainder when dividing by (x − c) equals f(c), a remainder of zero (i.e., f(c) = 0) means (x − c) divides evenly, making it a factor.

  Q20. What does it mean to 'test a candidate root' using the Factor Theorem?
    [ ] Multiply the candidate by the leading coefficient
    [*] Substitute the candidate into f(x) and check whether the result equals zero
    [ ] Graph the polynomial and look for the candidate on the y-axis
    [ ] Divide the constant term by the candidate
    Explanation: To test a candidate root c, evaluate f(c). If f(c) = 0, then c is a root and (x − c) is a factor. This can be done efficiently using synthetic division, where a remainder of 0 confirms the root.

  Q21. An engineer models the stress on a beam as S(x) = 2x³ − 7x² + 4x + 4, where x is the load factor. She wants to know if a load factor of x = 2 produces zero stress. What does the Remainder Theorem tell her?
    [*] S(2) = 0, so x = 2 produces zero stress and (x − 2) is a factor
    [ ] S(2) = 4, so there is some stress at x = 2
    [ ] The Remainder Theorem cannot be used for this type of problem
    [ ] S(2) = −2, so there is negative stress at x = 2
    Explanation: Evaluate S(2) = 2(8) − 7(4) + 4(2) + 4 = 16 − 28 + 8 + 4 = 0. Since S(2) = 0, the Remainder Theorem confirms zero stress at load factor 2, and the Factor Theorem tells us (x − 2) is a factor.

  Q22. A pharmacist models a drug concentration in mg/L as C(t) = t³ − 6t² + 11t − 6, where t is hours after administration. She tests t = 1 and finds C(1) = 0. After dividing out (t − 1), what is the depressed polynomial?
    [*] t² − 5t + 6
    [ ] t² + 5t − 6
    [ ] t² − 7t + 6
    [ ] t² − 6t + 11
    Explanation: Synthetic division of t³ − 6t² + 11t − 6 by (t − 1): bring down 1; 1·1 = 1, −6+1 = −5; 1·(−5) = −5, 11+(−5) = 6; 1·6 = 6, −6+6 = 0. The depressed polynomial is t² − 5t + 6 = (t − 2)(t − 3).

  Q23. A company models its revenue (in thousands of dollars) as R(x) = 3x³ + 2x² − 19x + 6, where x is the number of units sold (in hundreds). A consultant claims that x = 2 is a break-even point. Is this correct?
    [ ] Yes, because R(2) = 6
    [*] Yes, because R(2) = 0
    [ ] No, because R(2) = 8
    [ ] No, because R(2) = −6
    Explanation: R(2) = 3(8) + 2(4) − 19(2) + 6 = 24 + 8 − 38 + 6 = 0. Since R(2) = 0, the consultant is correct that x = 2 (200 units) is a break-even point.

  Q24. A student needs to find all rational roots of f(x) = 2x³ − 3x² − 8x + 12. She lists the candidates from the Rational Root Theorem and tests x = 2 first. What does she find?
    [ ] f(2) = 4, so x = 2 is not a root
    [ ] f(2) = 12, so x = 2 is not a root
    [ ] f(2) = −8, so x = 2 is not a root
    [*] f(2) = 0, so x = 2 is a root; she can then divide and solve the resulting quadratic
    Explanation: f(2) = 2(8) − 3(4) − 8(2) + 12 = 16 − 12 − 16 + 12 = 0. Since f(2) = 0, x = 2 is a root. Dividing gives 2x² + x − 6 = (2x − 3)(x + 2), so all roots are x = 2, x = 3/2, and x = −2.

  Q25. A physics teacher gives students f(x) = x⁴ − 5x³ + 5x² + 5x − 6 and asks them to find all roots. They discover that x = 1 and x = −1 are roots. After dividing out both factors, what quadratic remains?
    [*] x² − 5x + 6
    [ ] x² + x − 6
    [ ] x² + 5x − 6
    [ ] x² − 6x + 5
    Explanation: Divide f(x) by (x − 1) first: synthetic division gives x³ − 4x² + x + 6. Then divide by (x + 1): gives x² − 5x + 6 = (x − 2)(x − 3). So all four roots are x = 1, −1, 2, 3.

  Q26. A contractor models the volume of a custom planter as V(x) = x³ − 9x² + 26x − 24 cubic feet, where x is a design parameter. She needs the planter to have zero excess volume at a certain value. Using the Rational Root Theorem, which value of x works?
    [ ] x = 5, because V(5) = 125 − 225 + 130 − 24 = 6 ≠ 0
    [ ] x = 1, because V(1) = 1 − 9 + 26 − 24 = −6
    [ ] x = 6, because V(6) = 216 − 324 + 156 − 24 = 24
    [*] x = 2, because V(2) = 8 − 36 + 52 − 24 = 0
    Explanation: V(2) = 8 − 36 + 52 − 24 = 0. Since V(2) = 0, by the Factor Theorem (x − 2) is a factor. The complete factorization is V(x) = (x − 2)(x − 3)(x − 4), with roots at x = 2, 3, and 4.

  Q27. A quality control analyst models defect rates as D(x) = 2x³ − 3x² − 3x + 2, where x is a process parameter. She wants to verify that x = 1/2 is a value that produces zero defects. What should she do?
    [ ] Evaluate D(2) because the Rational Root Theorem uses the reciprocal
    [*] Evaluate D(1/2) = 2(1/8) − 3(1/4) − 3(1/2) + 2 = 0, confirming x = 1/2 is a root
    [ ] Evaluate D(1/2) = 1, so x = 1/2 is not a root
    [ ] The Rational Root Theorem does not include fractions as candidates
    Explanation: D(1/2) = 2(1/8) − 3(1/4) − 3(1/2) + 2 = 1/4 − 3/4 − 3/2 + 2 = 1/4 − 3/4 − 6/4 + 8/4 = 0. Since D(1/2) = 0, x = 1/2 is a root. The Rational Root Theorem does include fractions: candidates are ±(factors of 2)/(factors of 2) = ±1, ±2, ±1/2.

  Q28. A data scientist models web traffic flow as T(x) = x³ − 2x² − 5x + 6, where x is a scaling parameter. She uses the Rational Root Theorem to list candidates ±1, ±2, ±3, ±6, then tests each. Which values are actual roots?
    [ ] x = 1, x = 2, and x = 3
    [*] x = 1, x = −2, and x = 3
    [ ] x = 1 and x = 6
    [ ] x = −1, x = 2, and x = −3
    Explanation: T(1) = 1 − 2 − 5 + 6 = 0, so x = 1 is a root. Dividing gives x² − x − 6 = (x − 3)(x + 2). So the three roots are x = 1, x = 3, and x = −2.

  Q29. A city planner models a drainage function as D(x) = 2x⁴ − 5x³ − 4x² + 15x − 6, where x is a flow rate parameter. If x = 1/2 is a root, what is the depressed polynomial after dividing by (x − 1/2)?
    [ ] x³ − 2x² − 3x + 6
    [*] 2x³ − 4x² − 6x + 12
    [ ] 2x³ − 3x² − 5x + 6
    [ ] 2x³ − 6x² + 2x − 12
    Explanation: Synthetic division with c = 1/2 on coefficients 2, −5, −4, 15, −6: bring down 2; (1/2)(2) = 1, −5+1 = −4; (1/2)(−4) = −2, −4+(−2) = −6; (1/2)(−6) = −3, 15+(−3) = 12; (1/2)(12) = 6, −6+6 = 0. Quotient: 2x³ − 4x² − 6x + 12.

  Q30. An investment analyst models a fund's return as R(x) = x⁴ − 10x² + 9, where x is a market indicator. She factors by treating it as a quadratic in x². What are all the roots?
    [ ] x = 1 and x = 9
    [*] x = 1, −1, 3, −3
    [ ] x = 1 and x = 3 only
    [ ] x = ±1 only
    Explanation: Let u = x²: u² − 10u + 9 = (u − 1)(u − 9). Substituting back: (x² − 1)(x² − 9) = (x − 1)(x + 1)(x − 3)(x + 3). All four roots are x = 1, −1, 3, −3.

--- Lesson 3.5: Remainder & Factor Theorems (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Remainder theorem: f(x) = 2x³ + 5x - 3, remainder when divided by (x - 2)?
     A: f(2) = 16 + 10 - 3 = 23. Remainder is 23.

  2. Q: Use factor theorem: Is (x + 1) a factor of x³ + 2x² + x?
     A: f(-1) = -1 + 2 - 1 = 0. Yes, (x+1) is a factor.

  3. Q: Verify (x - 3) divides x⁴ - 3x³ - 4x + 12
     A: f(3) = 81 - 81 - 12 + 12 = 0. Yes, divisible.

  4. Q: If f(3) = 0, what factor appears in f(x)?
     A: (x - 3) is a factor of f(x).

  5. Q: Divide to find quotient when (x - 2) divides 2x⁴ + x³ - 7x + 2
     A: Use synthetic division with 2. Quotient: 2x³ + 5x² + 10x + 13, remainder 28.

  6. Q: Express f(x) = (x - 2)(x + 1) + 5 as division statement
     A: f(x) divided by (x-2)(x+1) gives quotient 1, remainder 5 + terms.

  7. Q: Remainder when 3x⁵ - 2x² + 7 divided by x + 2?
     A: f(-2) = -96 - 8 + 7 = -97. Remainder is -97.

  8. Q: Factor theorem consequence: if f(a) = 0, then?
     A: (x - a) is a factor AND (x - a) divides f(x) evenly.

  9. Q: What does remainder 0 indicate in division?
     A: Divisor is a factor. Polynomial divides evenly (no remainder).

  10. Q: Determine p such that (x - 2) divides x³ + px² - 4x - 8
     A: f(2) = 8 + 4p - 8 - 8 = 4p - 8 = 0, so p = 2.

--- Lesson 3.6: Complex Numbers & Polynomial Roots (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Complex Numbers & Polynomial Roots
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the conjugate of 4 − 7i?
The complex conjugate of a + bi is a − bi. For 4 − 7i, change the sign of the imaginary part to get 4 + 7i.
What is |3 + 4i|?
The modulus of a + bi is √(a² + b²). So |3 + 4i| = √(9 + 16) = √25 = 5.
What is i² equal to?
By definition, i = √(−1), so i² = −1. This is the fundamental property of the imaginary unit.
What is i⁴ equal to?
i⁴ = (i²)² = (−1)² = 1. The powers of i repeat in a cycle of 4: i¹ = i, i² = −1, i³ = −i, i⁴ = 1.
What is the imaginary unit i defined as?
The imaginary unit i is defined as √(−1). Its key property is that i² = −1, which extends the real number system to include solutions of equations like x² + 1 = 0.
What is the standard form of a complex number?
The standard form of a complex number is a + bi, where a is the real part and b is the imaginary part. Both a and b are real numbers. When b = 0, the number is real; when a = 0, it is purely imaginary.
What is a 'complex conjugate'?
The complex conjugate of a + bi is a − bi. The real part stays the same, while the sign of the imaginary part is reversed. Multiplying a complex number by its conjugate always gives a real number: (a + bi)(a − bi) = a² + b².
What is a 'purely imaginary' number?
A purely imaginary number has the form bi where b is a nonzero real number and the real part is 0. Examples include 3i, −5i, and i. When a = 0 and b ≠ 0, the complex number a + bi reduces to bi.
What is the 'complex plane' used for?
The complex plane (or Argand plane) represents complex numbers as points in two dimensions. The horizontal axis is the real axis and the vertical axis is the imaginary axis, so a + bi corresponds to the point (a, b).
What is the cycle of powers of i?
The powers of i follow a cycle of 4: i¹ = i, i² = −1, i³ = −i, i⁴ = 1, then i⁵ = i, and so on. To simplify iⁿ, divide n by 4 and use the remainder to determine the position in the cycle.
An electrical engineer works with impedance in a circuit represented by Z₁ = 5 + 3i ohms and Z₂ = 2 − 4i ohms. What is the total impedance Z₁ + Z₂ when the components are in series?
For series impedances, add them directly: (5 + 3i) + (2 − 4i) = (5 + 2) + (3i − 4i) = 7 − i ohms.
A mathematician knows that 1 + 2i is a root of a polynomial with real coefficients. She needs to write the quadratic factor that corresponds to this root and its conjugate. What is that factor?
The conjugate pair is 1 + 2i and 1 − 2i. The quadratic factor is [x − (1 + 2i)][x − (1 − 2i)] = (x − 1)² − (2i)² = x² − 2x + 1 + 4 = x² − 2x + 5.
A signal processing engineer multiplies two impedances: (3 + 2i)(1 − 4i). What is the result?
FOIL: (3)(1) + (3)(−4i) + (2i)(1) + (2i)(−4i) = 3 − 12i + 2i − 8i² = 3 − 10i − 8(−1) = 3 − 10i + 8 = 11 − 10i.
A student is told that a degree-3 polynomial with real coefficients has roots x = 4 and x = 1 − i. What is the third root?
By the Conjugate Root Theorem, complex roots of polynomials with real coefficients come in conjugate pairs. Since 1 − i is a root, its conjugate 1 + i must also be a root. The three roots are x = 4, x = 1 − i, and x = 1 + i.
An engineer divides voltage V = 10 + 5i by current I = 1 + 2i to find impedance Z = V/I. What is Z?
Multiply by conjugate: (10 + 5i)(1 − 2i)/[(1 + 2i)(1 − 2i)] = (10 − 20i + 5i − 10i²)/(1 + 4) = (10 − 15i + 10)/5 = (20 − 15i)/5 = 4 − 3i.
A quantum physics student needs to simplify i¹⁷. What is the result?
Divide the exponent by 4: 17 ÷ 4 = 4 remainder 1. Since the remainder is 1, i¹⁷ = i¹ = i. The cycle repeats every 4 powers: i, −1, −i, 1.
Real-World Applications
Example: A signal processing engineer multiplies two impedances: (3 + 2i)(1 − 4i). What is the result?
FOIL: (3)(1) + (3)(−4i) + (2i)(1) + (2i)(−4i) = 3 − 12i + 2i − 8i² = 3 − 10i − 8(−1) = 3 − 10i + 8 = 11 − 10i.
Example: An engineer divides voltage V = 10 + 5i by current I = 1 + 2i to find impedance Z = V/I. What is Z?
Multiply by conjugate: (10 + 5i)(1 − 2i)/[(1 + 2i)(1 − 2i)] = (10 − 20i + 5i − 10i²)/(1 + 4) = (10 − 15i + 10)/5 = (20 − 15i)/5 = 4 − 3i.

--- Lesson 3.6: Complex Numbers & Polynomial Roots (Quiz) ---

  Q1. Simplify: (3 + 2i) + (1 − 5i)
    [*] 4 − 3i
    [ ] 4 − 7i
    [ ] 2 + 7i
    [ ] 4 + 7i
    Explanation: Add the real parts: 3 + 1 = 4. Add the imaginary parts: 2i + (−5i) = −3i. The result is 4 − 3i.

  Q2. Simplify: (2 + i)(3 − i)
    [*] 7 + i
    [ ] 6 + 2i
    [ ] 5 − i
    [ ] 7 − i
    Explanation: Use FOIL: (2)(3) + (2)(−i) + (i)(3) + (i)(−i) = 6 − 2i + 3i − i² = 6 + i − (−1) = 6 + i + 1 = 7 + i.

  Q3. What is the conjugate of 4 − 7i?
    [ ] 7 − 4i
    [ ] −4 + 7i
    [ ] −4 − 7i
    [*] 4 + 7i
    Explanation: The complex conjugate of a + bi is a − bi. For 4 − 7i, change the sign of the imaginary part to get 4 + 7i.

  Q4. What is |3 + 4i|?
    [ ] 7
    [ ] 1
    [*] 5
    [ ] 25
    Explanation: The modulus of a + bi is √(a² + b²). So |3 + 4i| = √(9 + 16) = √25 = 5.

  Q5. Solve x² + 4 = 0.
    [*] x = ±2i
    [ ] x = ±2
    [ ] No solution
    [ ] x = ±4i
    Explanation: x² = −4, so x = ±√(−4) = ±2i. The equation has two complex solutions because the square of a real number cannot be negative.

  Q6. What is i² equal to?
    [ ] −i
    [ ] 1
    [ ] i
    [*] −1
    Explanation: By definition, i = √(−1), so i² = −1. This is the fundamental property of the imaginary unit.

  Q7. Simplify i³.
    [ ] −1
    [ ] 1
    [ ] i
    [*] −i
    Explanation: i³ = i² · i = (−1) · i = −i. The powers of i cycle: i, −1, −i, 1, i, −1, −i, 1, ...

  Q8. Simplify: (1 + i)²
    [ ] 1 + 2i
    [*] 2i
    [ ] 0
    [ ] 2
    Explanation: (1 + i)² = 1 + 2i + i² = 1 + 2i + (−1) = 2i.

  Q9. Simplify: (2 + 3i)(2 − 3i)
    [ ] −5
    [ ] 4 − 9i²
    [*] 13
    [ ] 4 + 9
    Explanation: Multiplying a complex number by its conjugate: (2 + 3i)(2 − 3i) = 4 − 9i² = 4 − 9(−1) = 4 + 9 = 13. The result is always a real number.

  Q10. What is i⁴ equal to?
    [*] 1
    [ ] −i
    [ ] −1
    [ ] i
    Explanation: i⁴ = (i²)² = (−1)² = 1. The powers of i repeat in a cycle of 4: i¹ = i, i² = −1, i³ = −i, i⁴ = 1.

  Q11. What is the imaginary unit i defined as?
    [ ] An undefined mathematical concept
    [ ] The number −1 itself
    [*] √(−1), where i² = −1
    [ ] The reciprocal of −1
    Explanation: The imaginary unit i is defined as √(−1). Its key property is that i² = −1, which extends the real number system to include solutions of equations like x² + 1 = 0.

  Q12. What is the standard form of a complex number?
    [ ] a × bi, where a and b are real numbers
    [ ] Only numbers of the form bi
    [ ] a + b, where a and b are integers
    [*] a + bi, where a and b are real numbers
    Explanation: The standard form of a complex number is a + bi, where a is the real part and b is the imaginary part. Both a and b are real numbers. When b = 0, the number is real; when a = 0, it is purely imaginary.

  Q13. What is a 'complex conjugate'?
    [ ] The conjugate only exists for purely imaginary numbers
    [ ] For a + bi, the conjugate is b + ai
    [ ] For a + bi, the conjugate is −a − bi
    [*] For a + bi, the conjugate is a − bi (same real part, opposite imaginary part)
    Explanation: The complex conjugate of a + bi is a − bi. The real part stays the same, while the sign of the imaginary part is reversed. Multiplying a complex number by its conjugate always gives a real number: (a + bi)(a − bi) = a² + b².

  Q14. What does the Conjugate Root Theorem state?
    [ ] If a + bi is a root, then −a + bi is also a root
    [ ] Every polynomial has conjugate roots
    [*] If a + bi is a root of a polynomial with real coefficients, then a − bi is also a root
    [ ] Complex roots always come in groups of four
    Explanation: The Conjugate Root Theorem states that for polynomials with real coefficients, complex roots always come in conjugate pairs. If a + bi (with b ≠ 0) is a root, then a − bi must also be a root.

  Q15. What does the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra guarantee?
    [ ] A degree-n polynomial always has n distinct real roots
    [ ] Polynomials of odd degree have no complex roots
    [*] A degree-n polynomial has exactly n roots (counting multiplicity) over the complex numbers
    [ ] Every polynomial can be factored using only integers
    Explanation: The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra states that every polynomial of degree n (with n >= 1) has exactly n roots in the complex number system, counting multiplicities. Some roots may be real, and complex roots come in conjugate pairs.

  Q16. What does the 'modulus' (or 'absolute value') of a complex number a + bi represent?
    [ ] The product a × b, particularly regarding value
    [*] The distance from the origin in the complex plane: √(a² + b²)
    [ ] The real part a only as typically measured in practice
    [ ] The sum a + b, particularly regarding value
    Explanation: The modulus |a + bi| = √(a² + b²) represents the distance from the complex number to the origin (0, 0) in the complex plane. It is always a non-negative real number.

  Q17. What is a 'purely imaginary' number?
    [ ] A number that cannot be computed
    [ ] A complex number where both parts are imaginary
    [ ] Any number that contains i
    [*] A complex number of the form bi, where the real part is zero
    Explanation: A purely imaginary number has the form bi where b is a nonzero real number and the real part is 0. Examples include 3i, −5i, and i. When a = 0 and b ≠ 0, the complex number a + bi reduces to bi.

  Q18. What does it mean to 'rationalize' a complex denominator?
    [ ] Replace i with 1 in the denominator
    [*] Multiply numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator to eliminate i
    [ ] Remove the imaginary part entirely
    [ ] Convert the complex number to a fraction
    Explanation: To divide complex numbers, multiply both the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator. This eliminates the imaginary unit from the denominator since (a + bi)(a − bi) = a² + b², a real number.

  Q19. What is the 'complex plane' used for?
    [ ] Solving only real-number equations
    [ ] Plotting only purely imaginary numbers
    [ ] Representing three-dimensional space
    [*] Graphing complex numbers with the real part on the x-axis and the imaginary part on the y-axis
    Explanation: The complex plane (or Argand plane) represents complex numbers as points in two dimensions. The horizontal axis is the real axis and the vertical axis is the imaginary axis, so a + bi corresponds to the point (a, b).

  Q20. What is the cycle of powers of i?
    [ ] i, 1, −i, −1, and then it repeats every 4 powers
    [*] i, −1, −i, 1, and then it repeats every 4 powers
    [ ] i, −i, 1, −1, repeating every 2 powers
    [ ] The powers of i never repeat
    Explanation: The powers of i follow a cycle of 4: i¹ = i, i² = −1, i³ = −i, i⁴ = 1, then i⁵ = i, and so on. To simplify iⁿ, divide n by 4 and use the remainder to determine the position in the cycle.

  Q21. An electrical engineer works with impedance in a circuit represented by Z₁ = 5 + 3i ohms and Z₂ = 2 − 4i ohms. What is the total impedance Z₁ + Z₂ when the components are in series?
    [*] 7 − i ohms
    [ ] 3 + 7i ohms
    [ ] 7 + 7i ohms
    [ ] 10 − 12i ohms
    Explanation: For series impedances, add them directly: (5 + 3i) + (2 − 4i) = (5 + 2) + (3i − 4i) = 7 − i ohms.

  Q22. A physics student solves the equation x² + 9 = 0 while studying oscillation frequencies. What are the solutions?
    [*] x = ±3i
    [ ] x = ±3
    [ ] No solutions exist
    [ ] x = ±9i
    Explanation: x² = −9, so x = ±√(−9) = ±3i. Although there are no real solutions, the complex number system provides two solutions: 3i and −3i.

  Q23. A mathematician knows that 1 + 2i is a root of a polynomial with real coefficients. She needs to write the quadratic factor that corresponds to this root and its conjugate. What is that factor?
    [*] x² − 2x + 5
    [ ] x² − 2x − 5
    [ ] x² + 5
    [ ] x² + 2x + 5
    Explanation: The conjugate pair is 1 + 2i and 1 − 2i. The quadratic factor is [x − (1 + 2i)][x − (1 − 2i)] = (x − 1)² − (2i)² = x² − 2x + 1 + 4 = x² − 2x + 5.

  Q24. A signal processing engineer multiplies two impedances: (3 + 2i)(1 − 4i). What is the result?
    [ ] −5 − 10i
    [*] 11 − 10i
    [ ] 3 − 8i
    [ ] 11 + 10i
    Explanation: FOIL: (3)(1) + (3)(−4i) + (2i)(1) + (2i)(−4i) = 3 − 12i + 2i − 8i² = 3 − 10i − 8(−1) = 3 − 10i + 8 = 11 − 10i.

  Q25. A student is told that a degree-3 polynomial with real coefficients has roots x = 4 and x = 1 − i. What is the third root?
    [*] x = 1 + i
    [ ] x = −1 + i
    [ ] x = −1 − i
    [ ] x = i − 1
    Explanation: By the Conjugate Root Theorem, complex roots of polynomials with real coefficients come in conjugate pairs. Since 1 − i is a root, its conjugate 1 + i must also be a root. The three roots are x = 4, x = 1 − i, and x = 1 + i.

  Q26. An engineer divides voltage V = 10 + 5i by current I = 1 + 2i to find impedance Z = V/I. What is Z?
    [*] 4 − 3i
    [ ] 2 + 5i
    [ ] 4 + 3i
    [ ] 10 + 5i
    Explanation: Multiply by conjugate: (10 + 5i)(1 − 2i)/[(1 + 2i)(1 − 2i)] = (10 − 20i + 5i − 10i²)/(1 + 4) = (10 − 15i + 10)/5 = (20 − 15i)/5 = 4 − 3i.

  Q27. A control systems analyst encounters the polynomial p(x) = x⁴ + 5x² + 4 and needs to find all roots. What are they?
    [ ] x = ±1 and x = ±2
    [*] x = ±i and x = ±2i
    [ ] x = ±i only
    [ ] No roots exist
    Explanation: Let u = x²: u² + 5u + 4 = (u + 1)(u + 4). So x² = −1 gives x = ±i and x² = −4 gives x = ±2i. All four roots are purely imaginary: ±i and ±2i.

  Q28. A quantum physics student needs to simplify i¹⁷. What is the result?
    [ ] −i
    [ ] −1
    [*] i
    [ ] 1
    Explanation: Divide the exponent by 4: 17 ÷ 4 = 4 remainder 1. Since the remainder is 1, i¹⁷ = i¹ = i. The cycle repeats every 4 powers: i, −1, −i, 1.

  Q29. A data scientist works with a degree-4 polynomial that has real coefficients. She finds two roots: x = 3 and x = −1. The discriminant of the remaining quadratic factor is negative. How many complex roots does the polynomial have?
    [ ] 4 complex roots
    [ ] 0 complex roots — all roots are real
    [*] 2 complex roots (a conjugate pair)
    [ ] 1 complex root
    Explanation: Two real roots (x = 3 and x = −1) account for 2 of the 4 total roots. The remaining quadratic has a negative discriminant, which means 2 complex conjugate roots. So the polynomial has 2 real and 2 complex roots.

  Q30. An acoustics engineer models a vibration equation whose characteristic polynomial is p(x) = x³ − 3x² + 4x − 12. She finds that x = 3 is a root. What are all the roots?
    [ ] x = 3, x = 4i, x = −4i
    [ ] x = 3 only (repeated root)
    [*] x = 3, x = 2i, x = −2i
    [ ] x = 3, x = 2, x = −2
    Explanation: Divide x³ − 3x² + 4x − 12 by (x − 3): synthetic division gives x² + 4. Setting x² + 4 = 0 gives x² = −4, so x = ±2i. All three roots are x = 3, x = 2i, and x = −2i.

--- Lesson 3.6: Complex Numbers & Polynomial Roots (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Add (3 + 2i) + (1 - 5i)
     A: 4 - 3i. Add real and imaginary parts separately.

  2. Q: Multiply (2 + i)(3 - i)
     A: 7 + i. FOIL: 6 - 2i + 3i - i² = 6 + i + 1.

  3. Q: Find conjugate of 4 - 7i
     A: 4 + 7i. Change sign of imaginary part.

  4. Q: Divide (5 + 2i)/(1 - i)
     A: (7 + 7i)/2 = 3.5 + 3.5i. Multiply by conjugate: (5+2i)(1+i)/((1-i)(1+i)).

  5. Q: Find |3 + 4i| (magnitude)
     A: 5. √(9 + 16) = √25 = 5.

  6. Q: Simplify i⁴
     A: 1. i² = -1, so i⁴ = (i²)² = 1.

  7. Q: Solve x² + 4 = 0
     A: x = ±2i. x² = -4, x = ±√(-4) = ±2i.

  8. Q: Write in a + bi form: (1 + i)/(1 - i)
     A: i. Multiply by conjugate: (1+i)²/(1-(-1)) = 2i/2 = i.

  9. Q: If z = 2 - 3i, find z·z̄ (z times conjugate)
     A: 13. (2-3i)(2+3i) = 4 + 9 = 13.

  10. Q: Quadratic x² - 2x + 5 = 0 has complex roots?
     A: Yes: x = (2 ± √(4-20))/2 = (2 ± 4i)/2 = 1 ± 2i.

--- Lesson 3.7: Higher-Degree Polynomials (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Higher-Degree Polynomials
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is the maximum number of real zeros a quartic (degree-4) polynomial can have?
A polynomial of degree n can have at most n real zeros. A quartic (degree 4) can have at most 4 real zeros.
What is the end behavior of f(x) = x⁶ − 3x⁴ + 2?
The leading term is x⁶. Even degree (6) with positive leading coefficient (1) means both ends of the graph go to +∞.
What is Descartes' Rule of Signs?
Descartes' Rule of Signs states that the number of positive real zeros equals the number of sign changes in f(x), or less by a multiple of 2. To find negative real zeros, apply the rule to f(−x).
What is a 'quartic' polynomial?
A quartic polynomial has degree 4 (highest exponent is 4). The naming convention by degree is: linear (1), quadratic (2), cubic (3), quartic (4), quintic (5).
What is a 'quintic' polynomial?
A quintic polynomial has degree 5 (highest exponent is 5). Unlike quadratics, cubics, and quartics, there is no general formula using radicals for solving quintic equations (proven by the Abel-Ruffini theorem).
What is the Lower Bound Test in synthetic division?
The Lower Bound Test states that if c < 0 and synthetic division produces a bottom row with alternating signs (positive/negative/positive/..., with 0 counting as either), then no real zero is less than c.
What is the systematic approach to finding all roots of a higher-degree polynomial?
The systematic approach combines the Rational Root Theorem (to list candidates), synthetic division (to test and factor), and repeated degree reduction until a quadratic (or factorable polynomial) is reached, which can then be solved directly.
A production manager models daily output with P(x) = 2x⁵ − 3x⁴ − 12x³ + 18x² + 16x − 24. She discovers that x = 1 is NOT a root but x = −2 is. After dividing by (x + 2), what is the degree of the remaining polynomial?
Dividing a degree-5 polynomial by a linear factor (x + 2) produces a quotient of degree 5 − 1 = 4. The process of degree reduction continues until all roots are found or a quadratic remains.
Real-World Applications
Example: By the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra, how many total roots (counting multiplicity, over the complex numbers) does a degree-4 polynomial have?
The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra guarantees that a degree-n polynomial has exactly n roots over the complex numbers when counting multiplicity.
Example: A production manager models daily output with P(x) = 2x⁵ − 3x⁴ − 12x³ + 18x² + 16x − 24. She discovers that x = 1 is NOT a root but x = −2 is. After dividing by (x + 2), what is the degree of the remaining polynomial?
Dividing a degree-5 polynomial by a linear factor (x + 2) produces a quotient of degree 5 − 1 = 4. The process of degree reduction continues until all roots are found or a quadratic remains.

--- Lesson 3.7: Higher-Degree Polynomials (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What are the possible rational roots of f(x) = 2x³ − 3x + 1?
    [ ] ±1, ±2
    [ ] ±2, ±3
    [*] ±1, ±1/2
    [ ] ±1, ±3
    Explanation: By the Rational Root Theorem, possible roots are ±(factors of constant term)/(factors of leading coefficient) = ±(factors of 1)/(factors of 2) = ±1, ±1/2.

  Q2. Test x = 1/2 in f(x) = 2x³ + x² − 5x + 2. Is x = 1/2 a root?
    [ ] No, f(1/2) = −2
    [*] Yes, f(1/2) = 0
    [ ] Cannot be determined
    [ ] No, f(1/2) = 1
    Explanation: f(1/2) = 2(1/8) + (1/4) − 5(1/2) + 2 = 1/4 + 1/4 − 5/2 + 2 = 1/2 − 5/2 + 2 = −2 + 2 = 0. Since f(1/2) = 0, x = 1/2 is a root.

  Q3. Using Descartes' Rule of Signs, how many possible positive real zeros does f(x) = x³ − 2x² + x − 1 have?
    [ ] 2 or 0
    [*] 3 or 1
    [ ] Exactly 1
    [ ] Exactly 3
    Explanation: Count sign changes in f(x): + to − (1st), − to + (2nd), + to − (3rd). There are 3 sign changes, so there are 3 or 1 positive real zeros (subtract by 2 each time).

  Q4. If f(1) > 0 and f(2) < 0, what does the Intermediate Value Theorem guarantee?
    [ ] The function is undefined between x = 1 and x = 2
    [*] There is at least one real root between x = 1 and x = 2
    [ ] There is no root between x = 1 and x = 2
    [ ] There is a root at x = 0
    Explanation: The Intermediate Value Theorem states that if a continuous function changes sign over an interval (f(1) > 0 and f(2) < 0), it must cross zero at least once in that interval.

  Q5. By the Fundamental Theorem of Algebra, how many total roots (counting multiplicity, over the complex numbers) does a degree-4 polynomial have?
    [*] Exactly 4
    [ ] At most 4
    [ ] 2 to 4
    [ ] 0 to 4
    Explanation: The Fundamental Theorem of Algebra guarantees that a degree-n polynomial has exactly n roots over the complex numbers when counting multiplicity.

  Q6. Factor x⁴ − 5x² + 4 completely.
    [*] (x − 1)(x + 1)(x − 2)(x + 2)
    [ ] (x − 4)(x − 1)
    [ ] (x² − 1)(x² + 4)
    [ ] (x² − 4)(x² + 1)
    Explanation: Treat as a quadratic in u = x²: u² − 5u + 4 = (u − 1)(u − 4) = (x² − 1)(x² − 4). Factor further: (x − 1)(x + 1)(x − 2)(x + 2).

  Q7. What is the maximum number of real zeros a quartic (degree-4) polynomial can have?
    [*] 4
    [ ] 5
    [ ] 8
    [ ] 3
    Explanation: A polynomial of degree n can have at most n real zeros. A quartic (degree 4) can have at most 4 real zeros.

  Q8. In the Upper Bound Test using synthetic division with c > 0, if all values in the last row are non-negative, what can you conclude?
    [ ] The polynomial has no real roots
    [ ] c is a root of the polynomial
    [*] There are no real zeros greater than c
    [ ] All roots are positive
    Explanation: The Upper Bound Test states that if c > 0 and all entries in the bottom row of synthetic division are non-negative, then c is an upper bound for the real zeros of the polynomial.

  Q9. A degree-5 polynomial must have at least how many real zeros?
    [*] 1
    [ ] 0
    [ ] 3
    [ ] 5
    Explanation: Odd-degree polynomials must have at least one real zero because the end behaviors are opposite (one end goes to +∞ and the other to −∞), so the graph must cross the x-axis at least once.

  Q10. What is the end behavior of f(x) = x⁶ − 3x⁴ + 2?
    [ ] Both ends go to −∞
    [*] Both ends go to +∞
    [ ] Left goes to +∞, right goes to −∞
    [ ] Left goes to −∞, right goes to +∞
    Explanation: The leading term is x⁶. Even degree (6) with positive leading coefficient (1) means both ends of the graph go to +∞.

  Q11. What is Descartes' Rule of Signs?
    [*] A method that counts sign changes in f(x) to estimate the number of positive real zeros
    [ ] A method for determining the degree of a polynomial
    [ ] A formula for finding all roots of a polynomial
    [ ] A test for whether a polynomial has complex roots
    Explanation: Descartes' Rule of Signs states that the number of positive real zeros equals the number of sign changes in f(x), or less by a multiple of 2. To find negative real zeros, apply the rule to f(−x).

  Q12. What is a 'quartic' polynomial?
    [ ] A polynomial of degree 3
    [ ] A polynomial of degree 5
    [*] A polynomial of degree 4
    [ ] A polynomial with 4 terms
    Explanation: A quartic polynomial has degree 4 (highest exponent is 4). The naming convention by degree is: linear (1), quadratic (2), cubic (3), quartic (4), quintic (5).

  Q13. How do you use Descartes' Rule of Signs to estimate the number of negative real zeros?
    [ ] Count the negative coefficients in f(x)
    [*] Count sign changes in f(−x), then subtract multiples of 2
    [ ] Count sign changes in f(x) and negate the result
    [ ] Evaluate f(−1) and check if it equals zero
    Explanation: To estimate negative real zeros, substitute −x for x in the polynomial to get f(−x), then count the sign changes. The number of negative real zeros equals the sign changes or less by a multiple of 2.

  Q14. What does it mean for a polynomial to be 'irreducible over the reals'?
    [*] It cannot be factored into polynomials with real coefficients of lower degree
    [ ] It has no roots at all, particularly regarding factored under
    [ ] It has only one term, particularly regarding over under the
    [ ] It is a constant polynomial when considering all relevant factors
    Explanation: A polynomial that is irreducible over the reals cannot be factored into polynomials of lower degree with real coefficients. For example, x² + 1 is irreducible over the reals because its roots are ±i (complex).

  Q15. What is a 'quintic' polynomial?
    [*] A polynomial of degree 5
    [ ] A polynomial with 5 real roots
    [ ] A polynomial of degree 4
    [ ] A polynomial with 5 terms
    Explanation: A quintic polynomial has degree 5 (highest exponent is 5). Unlike quadratics, cubics, and quartics, there is no general formula using radicals for solving quintic equations (proven by the Abel-Ruffini theorem).

  Q16. What does 'polynomial identity' mean?
    [ ] An equation that is true for exactly one value
    [ ] A polynomial that equals zero
    [ ] Two polynomials that have the same degree
    [*] An equation involving polynomials that is true for all values of the variable
    Explanation: A polynomial identity is an equation that holds true for every value of the variable. For example, (a + b)² = a² + 2ab + b² is an identity because it is valid regardless of what values a and b take.

  Q17. What is the Lower Bound Test in synthetic division?
    [ ] If c < 0 and all last-row values are positive, then c is a lower bound
    [*] If c < 0 and the last row alternates in sign, then c is a lower bound for the real zeros
    [ ] It only works for quadratic polynomials
    [ ] The smallest root is always c
    Explanation: The Lower Bound Test states that if c < 0 and synthetic division produces a bottom row with alternating signs (positive/negative/positive/..., with 0 counting as either), then no real zero is less than c.

  Q18. What does 'quadratic in form' (or 'quadratic substitution') mean for higher-degree polynomials?
    [ ] A cubic polynomial written in standard form
    [ ] Any polynomial that has exactly two terms
    [*] A polynomial like ax⁴ + bx² + c that can be solved by substituting u = x²
    [ ] A polynomial that can only be solved by graphing
    Explanation: A polynomial is 'quadratic in form' when it can be written as au² + bu + c where u is some expression in x (often u = x²). This substitution reduces the higher-degree polynomial to a standard quadratic equation.

  Q19. What is the systematic approach to finding all roots of a higher-degree polynomial?
    [*] List rational candidates, test using synthetic division, reduce degree, repeat until all roots found
    [ ] Factor by grouping only
    [ ] Use the quadratic formula directly on the polynomial
    [ ] Graph the polynomial and estimate the x-intercepts
    Explanation: The systematic approach combines the Rational Root Theorem (to list candidates), synthetic division (to test and factor), and repeated degree reduction until a quadratic (or factorable polynomial) is reached, which can then be solved directly.

  Q20. What does 'degree reduction' mean when solving polynomial equations?
    [ ] Lowering all exponents by 1
    [*] After finding a root, dividing out the corresponding factor to get a polynomial of lower degree
    [ ] Converting a polynomial into a linear function
    [ ] Removing the constant term from the polynomial
    Explanation: Degree reduction is the process of dividing a polynomial by a known linear factor (x − r) after finding a root r, producing a quotient polynomial of one lower degree that can be analyzed further.

  Q21. A structural engineer analyzes a support beam whose stress function is S(x) = x⁴ − 5x³ + 5x² + 5x − 6, where x is a load parameter. Using the Rational Root Theorem, she tests x = 1. After confirming it is a root and dividing, what polynomial remains?
    [*] x³ − 4x² + x + 6
    [ ] x³ − 6x² + 5x + 5
    [ ] x³ + 4x² − x − 6
    [ ] x³ − 5x² + 5x − 6
    Explanation: S(1) = 1 − 5 + 5 + 5 − 6 = 0, confirming x = 1 is a root. Synthetic division with c = 1 on coefficients 1, −5, 5, 5, −6 gives quotient x³ − 4x² + x + 6.

  Q22. A pharmacist models drug concentration over time with C(t) = 2t⁴ − 7t³ + 3t² + 8t − 4. She uses Descartes' Rule of Signs. How many sign changes occur in C(t)?
    [ ] 1 sign change, so there is exactly 1 positive real zero
    [ ] 2 sign changes, so there are 2 or 0 positive real zeros
    [ ] 4 sign changes, so there are 4 or 2 or 0 positive real zeros
    [*] 3 sign changes, so there are 3 or 1 positive real zeros
    Explanation: The coefficients are +2, −7, +3, +8, −4. Sign changes: +2 to −7 (1st), −7 to +3 (2nd), +3 to +8 (no change), +8 to −4 (3rd). There are 3 sign changes, meaning 3 or 1 positive real zeros.

  Q23. A population biologist models a growth equation as P(x) = x⁵ − 2x⁴ − x + 2. She tries to factor by grouping. What factored form does she find?
    [ ] The polynomial cannot be factored by grouping
    [*] (x⁴ − 1)(x − 2) = (x − 1)(x + 1)(x² + 1)(x − 2)
    [ ] (x⁴ + 1)(x − 2)
    [ ] (x³ − 1)(x² − 2)
    Explanation: Group: x⁴(x − 2) − 1(x − 2) = (x⁴ − 1)(x − 2). Factor x⁴ − 1 as a difference of squares: (x² − 1)(x² + 1) = (x − 1)(x + 1)(x² + 1). Real roots are x = 1, −1, 2; plus two complex roots from x² + 1 = 0.

  Q24. A financial analyst models an investment return as R(x) = x⁴ − 10x² + 9. She recognizes this is quadratic in form. What substitution should she use, and what are all the real roots?
    [ ] Let u = x²; roots are x = 1 and x = 9
    [ ] This polynomial is not quadratic in form
    [ ] Let u = x; roots are x = ±1 only
    [*] Let u = x²; roots are x = ±1, ±3
    Explanation: Let u = x²: u² − 10u + 9 = (u − 1)(u − 9). Then x² = 1 gives x = ±1, and x² = 9 gives x = ±3. All four real roots are x = 1, −1, 3, −3.

  Q25. A civil engineer uses f(x) = 3x⁴ + 5x³ − 2x² − 5x + 2 to model a bridge load scenario. What are all the rational root candidates from the Rational Root Theorem?
    [ ] ±1, ±2, ±3
    [ ] ±1, ±2 only
    [ ] ±1, ±3, ±5
    [*] ±1, ±2, ±1/3, ±2/3
    Explanation: By the Rational Root Theorem: possible roots = ±(factors of 2)/(factors of 3) = ±1, ±2, ±1/3, ±2/3. These are candidates to test using synthetic division or direct substitution.

  Q26. A chemistry student encounters the equation x⁶ − 9x³ + 8 = 0 and is told it is quadratic in form. What substitution reduces it to a quadratic, and what are the real solutions?
    [*] Let u = x³; u² − 9u + 8 = (u − 1)(u − 8), so x = 1 and x = 2
    [ ] The equation cannot be made quadratic in form
    [ ] Let u = x³; u² − 9u + 8 = 0, so x = 1 only
    [ ] Let u = x²; solutions are x = ±1 and x = ±8
    Explanation: Let u = x³: u² − 9u + 8 = (u − 1)(u − 8) = 0. So u = 1 giving x³ = 1, x = 1, and u = 8 giving x³ = 8, x = 2. The two real solutions are x = 1 and x = 2.

  Q27. A data analyst encounters f(x) = x⁴ − 3x³ + x − 5 and applies Descartes' Rule of Signs. The coefficients are +1, −3, 0, +1, −5. How many sign changes are there?
    [ ] 3 sign changes (positive to negative, skip zero, negative to positive, positive to negative)
    [*] 3 sign changes, so 3 or 1 positive real zeros
    [ ] 4 sign changes, so 4 or 2 or 0 positive real zeros
    [ ] 2 sign changes, so 2 or 0 positive real zeros
    Explanation: List nonzero coefficients in order: +1, −3, +1, −5. Sign changes: + to − (1st), − to + (2nd), + to − (3rd). There are 3 sign changes, so there are 3 or 1 positive real zeros. Zero coefficients are skipped when counting.

  Q28. A robotics engineer models a joint's angular displacement as θ(t) = t⁵ + 2t⁴ − 13t³ − 14t² + 24t. She factors out t first, then needs to find the remaining roots. What does factoring out t give?
    [ ] t(t⁴ + 2t³ − 13t² − 14t − 24)
    [ ] t(t⁴ − 13t² + 24)
    [*] t(t⁴ + 2t³ − 13t² − 14t + 24)
    [ ] t²(t³ + 2t² − 13t − 14)
    Explanation: Factor out the common factor t: t(t⁴ + 2t³ − 13t² − 14t + 24). This gives t = 0 as one root, and the remaining roots come from the degree-4 polynomial, which can be solved using the Rational Root Theorem.

  Q29. A production manager models daily output with P(x) = 2x⁵ − 3x⁴ − 12x³ + 18x² + 16x − 24. She discovers that x = 1 is NOT a root but x = −2 is. After dividing by (x + 2), what is the degree of the remaining polynomial?
    [ ] 2
    [ ] 3
    [*] 4
    [ ] 5
    Explanation: Dividing a degree-5 polynomial by a linear factor (x + 2) produces a quotient of degree 5 − 1 = 4. The process of degree reduction continues until all roots are found or a quadratic remains.

  Q30. An environmental scientist models pollutant decay as C(t) = t⁴ + 2t³ − 7t² − 8t + 12. She finds t = 1 and t = 2 are roots. After dividing both out, she gets a quadratic t² + 5t + 6. What are all four roots of C(t)?
    [ ] t = 1, t = 2, t = 3, t = −2
    [ ] t = 1, t = 2, t = −1, t = −6
    [ ] t = 1, t = 2, t = 2, t = 3
    [*] t = 1, t = 2, t = −2, t = −3
    Explanation: The remaining quadratic t² + 5t + 6 = (t + 2)(t + 3) gives t = −2 and t = −3. All four roots are t = 1, 2, −2, −3. We can verify: C(t) = (t − 1)(t − 2)(t + 2)(t + 3).

--- Lesson 3.7: Higher-Degree Polynomials (Flashcards) ---

  1. Q: Rational Root Theorem: possible rational roots of 2x³ - 3x + 1?
     A: ±1, ±1/2. Factors of constant 1: ±1. Factors of leading 2: ±1, ±2.

  2. Q: Test x = 1/2 in 2x³ + x² - 5x + 2
     A: f(1/2) = 1/4 + 1/4 - 5/2 + 2 = 0. Yes, x = 1/2 is a root.

  3. Q: Descartes' Rule of Signs: x³ - 2x² + x - 1, positive roots?
     A: 3 or 1 positive roots. Three sign changes: +x³ to -2x² to +x to -1.

  4. Q: Number of possible negative roots using Descartes' Rule: f(-x)?
     A: Count sign changes in f(-x). (-x)³ - 2(-x)² + (-x) - 1 = -x³ - 2x² - x - 1: 0 sign changes = 0 negative roots.

  5. Q: Test p/q = -3/2 in 2x³ + 5x² - 9x - 18
     A: f(-3/2) = -27/4 + 45/4 + 27/2 - 18 = ... (calculate).

  6. Q: If x³ - 5x² + 8x - 4 has factor (x - 1), other factors?
     A: (x - 1)² (x - 4). (x-1) has multiplicity 2.

  7. Q: Bounds for real roots: if f(1) > 0 and f(2) < 0
     A: Root between 1 and 2 (Intermediate Value Theorem).

  8. Q: Upper/lower bounds test: Is x = 2 an upper bound for zeros of x³ - 3x + 1?
     A: Synthetic division: check if all signs positive (upper). 2: 1, 2, 1, 3. All ≥ 0, so upper bound.

  9. Q: Total roots (real and complex) of degree 4 polynomial
     A: Exactly 4 roots (counting multiplicity). Fundamental Theorem of Algebra.

  10. Q: If 2 + i is a root of polynomial with real coefficients, other root?
     A: 2 - i. Complex roots come in conjugate pairs for real polynomials.
